The Three Types of Denotation: Mutabaqa, Tadmin, Iltizam
Author explains the three types of linguistic denotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each type of denotation in a given word and explain the logical relationships between them.

Core Idea
Words can indicate three things: the full meaning, a part, or an external necessary association.

When we hear a word, what exactly does it mean? In logic, a word can indicate three distinct things: the complete meaning it was coined for, a part of that meaning, or something necessarily connected to that meaning. This lesson unpacks these three types—mutabaqa, tadmin, iltizam—and shows how they are related. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for precise reasoning and interpretation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the complete meaning (mutabaqa)
For any given word, determine the entire concept for which the word was placed. For example, 'house' (bayt) by mutabaqa indicates the whole structure, not just a room.
Mutabaqa is the default denotation; every word has mutabaqa for its complete meaning.
2
Identify the part (tadmin)
Check if the word also indicates a part of that complete meaning. For 'house', it indicates a room (part of the house). The author notes that tadmin is necessary in compounds (compound words have parts).
Tadmin is valid only if the whole has parts; simple words may not have tadmin.
3
Identify external associations (iltizam)
Determine if the word indicates something outside its meaning that is necessarily associated with it. For 'house', it implies a builder (by customary relation). The author requires a mental or customary relation for iltizam.
Iltizam depends on a real connection, not arbitrary association.
4
Check the relationships between the three types
The author states that mutabaqa is necessary for both tadmin and iltizam (if tadmin or iltizam hold, mutabaqa must hold). However, tadmin and iltizam are independent: having one does not require the other.
Also, mutabaqa can exist without tadmin or iltizam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَدَلَالَهُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى تَمَام مَا وُضِعَ لَهُ مِنْ تِلْكَ الْحَيْنِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةٌ، وَعَلَى جُزْيْهِ تَضَمُّنٌ ، وَهُوَ لَازِمٌ لَهَا فِي الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ ، وَعَلَى الْخَارِجِ الْتِزَامُ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ عَلَاقَةٍ عَفْلِيَّةٍ أَوْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ، وَقَدْ قِيلَ : الاِلْتِرَامُ مَهْجُورٌ في الْعُلُوم؛ لأَنَّهُ عَفْلِيٌّ، وَنُقِضَ بالتّضَمَّن وَيَلْزَمُهُمَا الْمُطَابَقَةُ، وَلَا عَكْسَ، وَكَوْنُهُ لَيْسَ غَيْرَهُ لَيْسَ مِمَّا يَسْبِقُ ، إِلَيْهِ الذَّهْنُ دَائِمًا ، وَأَمَّا التَّضَمُّنِيَّةُ وَالِالْتِزَامِيَّةُ فَلَا لُزُومَ بَيْنَهُمَا .
So the word's indication of the entirety of what it is placed for from that [aspect] is mutabaqa (correspondence), and of its part is tadmin (inclusion), and that is necessary for it in compounds, and of external [meaning] is iltizam (implication), and there must be a mental or customary relation. And it has been said: iltizam is abandoned in the sciences because it is mental, and it is refuted by tadmin and mutabaqa is necessary for both, and not vice versa, and its not being other than it is not something the mind always precedes to. As for tadmin and iltizam, there is no necessity between them.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of denotation for words. The author explains that when a word is placed for a meaning, it indicates that meaning in one of three ways: (1) mutabaqa, indicating the entire meaning for which it was placed; (2) tadmin, indicating a part of that meaning; and (3) iltizam, indicating something external that is necessarily associated with the meaning. For example, the word 'house' (bayt) indicates the whole structure by mutabaqa, a room by tadmin, and the builder by iltizam. The author notes that tadmin necessarily occurs in compound words (since every compound has parts). He then mentions a critique that iltizam is abandoned because it relies on mental association, but he defends it by noting that tadmin also involves a part-whole relation, and that mutabaqa holds for both. He clarifies that there is no necessary relationship between tadmin and iltizam; they are independent types.

Key Terms

تضمن
التزام

Checkpoint
Which type of denotation does the word 'human' (insan) have when it indicates 'animal' (hayawan)?
A) Mutabaqa
B) Tadmin
C) Iltizam
D) None

Key Takeaway
Mutabaqa is the primary denotation; tadmin and iltizam are secondary and independent of each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the word 'zakat' (zakah). Identify what it indicates by mutabaqa, tadmin, and iltizam. For each, provide a concrete example based on the definitions in this lesson.
?
Consider the word 'fasting' (sawm). Does it have tadmin? Does it have iltizam? Explain your reasoning using the relationships taught in this lesson.

Logical Division of Words: Mufrad, Murakkab, and the Three Types
Author defines mufrad and murakkab and subdivides mufrad into ism, fi'l, and harf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) words according to the logicians, and correctly classify a simple word as noun (ism), verb (kalima), or particle (harf) based on its logical properties.

Core Idea
The logician's classification of words differs from the grammarian's; truth-value and time-indication are key.

The author sets out the fundamental division of spoken expressions into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) based on whether a part of the word indicates a part of its meaning. He then subdivides the simple word into three categories: noun (ism), verb (kalima), and particle (harf/adaa). This classification is not identical to the grammarian's, as the logician focuses on truth-bearing capacity and function in predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for compound vs simple
If a part of the word indicates a part of its meaning, it is compound (murakkab); otherwise it is simple (mufrad).
For example, 'kitab' is simple because its parts 'ki', 'ta', 'ab' do not individually indicate parts of 'book'.
2
Recognize the three types of simple words
A simple word is either a particle (adaa) that serves as a mirror for another's state, a verb (kalima) that indicates time and can be true/false, or a noun (ism) that can be a subject of predication.
3
Distinguish logical verb (kalima) from grammatical verb (fi'l)
Not all verbs for grammarians are kalima for logicians. For instance, 'amshi' (I walk) is a grammatical verb but not a logical verb because it does not have a definite truth-value context.
The author explicitly contrasts 'amshi' and 'yamshi' to illustrate this.
4
Understand the particle as a 'mirror'
A particle, like 'min' (from), does not have an independent meaning but indicates the state of another word.
This is different from the grammatical classification where particles are simply functors.
5
Identify the noun by the property of being predicated
Nouns can be subjects of judgments (al-hukm 'alayhi), which distinguishes them from verbs and particles.
For example, 'Zaid' can be the subject of 'Zaidun qa'imun' (Zaid is standing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإقْرَادُ وَالتَّرْكِيبُ حَقِيقَةٌ صِفَةُ اللَّفْظِ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنْ دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزءٍ مَعْنَاهُ فَمُرَكَّبٌ ، وَيُسَمَّى قَوْلًا وَمُؤَلَّفا ، وَإِلَّا فَمُفْرَدٌ، وَهُوَ إِنْ كَانَ مِرْأَةٌ لِتَعَرُّفِ حَالِ الْغَيْرِ فَقَطْ فَأَدَاةٌ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْكَلِمَاتِ الْوُجُودِيَّةَ مِنْهَا، فَإِنَّ «كَانَ مَثَلًا مَعْنَاهُ كَوْنُ الشَّيْء شَيْئًا لَمْ يُذْكَرُ بَعْدُ، وَتَسْمِيَتُهَا كَلِمَاتٍ لِتَصَرُّفَهَا وَدَلَالَتِهَا عَلَى الزَّمَانِ، وَإِلَّا فإنْ دَلَّ بِهَيْئَتِهِ عَلَى زَمَانٍ فَكَلِمَةٌ. وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ فِعْلٍ عِنْدَ الْعَرَبِ كَلِمَةٌ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ؛ فَإِنَّ نَحْوَ «أَمْشِي» وتَمْشِي مَثَلًا فِعْل عِنْدَهُمْ ، وَلَيْسَ بِكَلِمَةٍ عِنْدَ الْمَنْطِقِيِّينَ ؛ لِاحْتِمَالِهِ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ، بِخِلَافِ يَمْشِي، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ اسْمٌ، وَمِنْ خَوَاصٌهِ الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ، وَقَوْلُهُمْ مِنْ حَرْفُ جَرٌ وضَرَبَ فِعْلٌ مَاضِ» لَا يَرِدُ؛ فَإِنَّهُ حُكْمٌ عَلَىٰ نَفْسٍ الصَّوْتِ ، لَا عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ، وَالْمُخْتَصُّ بِهِ هُوَ هَذَا، وَالْأَوَّلُ ، يَجْرِي فِي الْمُهْمَلَاتِ أَيْضًا.
Singularity and composition are truly attributes of the word, because if its part indicates a part of its meaning, then it is compound (murakkab), and it is called an utterance (qawl) and a composition (mu'allaf). Otherwise it is simple (mufrad). And if it is only a mirror for knowing the state of something else, then it is a particle (adaa). The correct view is that existential words (like 'kana' meaning 'was') are among them, for example 'kana' means the being of something not yet mentioned, and they are called words (kalimat) due to their conjugation and indication of time. Otherwise if it indicates time by its form, then it is a verb (kalima). Not every verb (fi'l) according to Arabs is a word (kalima) for the logicians; for example 'amshi' and 'tamshi' are verbs for them, but not words for the logicians because they admit truth and falsehood, unlike 'yamshi'. Otherwise it is a noun (ism), and among its properties is being predicated. And their saying 'min is a particle of jar, and daraba is a past verb' is not a counterexample, because that is a judgment on the sound itself, not on its meaning. And the first [type] also applies to meaningless words.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the division of words into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). The author states that if a word's part indicates a part of its meaning, it is compound; otherwise it is simple. He then subdivides the simple word: if it only serves to indicate the state of something else (like a tool), it is a particle (adaa); if it indicates time by its form, it is a verb (kalima); otherwise it is a noun (ism). He clarifies that the logician's classification differs from the grammarian's: for example, 'amshi' (I walk) is a verb for grammarians but not for logicians because it can be true or false; logicians only consider verbs that are declarative (like 'yamshi' he walks) as words (kalimat) because they can take truth value. He also explains that a noun is characterized by being a subject of predication. The author addresses a potential objection: saying 'min is a particle' or 'daraba is a verb' is a judgment on the word itself, not its meaning, so it doesn't affect the definitions.

Key Terms
مفرد
مركب
كلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a 'kalima' (logical verb)?
A) 'qara'a' (he read)
B) 'aqra'u' (I read)
C) 'qalam' (pen)
D) 'fi' (in)

Key Takeaway
The logician's division of words into simple and compound, and further into noun, verb, and particle, is based on meaning and truth-value, not just grammatical form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following words as simple or compound according to the logician's criterion: 'kitab' (book), 'rasul Allah' (Messenger of Allah), 'min' (from), 'yaqra'u' (he reads). For the simple ones, determine whether each is a noun (ism), verb (kalima), or particle (harf), and justify using the criteria from the passage.
?
Identify which of the following grammatical verbs would be considered a kalima by the logician: 'kataba' (he wrote), 'aktubu' (I write), 'taktubu' (you write). Explain your reasoning based on the passage's discussion of 'amshi' and 'yamshi'.

Mushtarak and Murtajal
Author defines mushtarak and murtajal, affirming mushtarak occurs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mushtarak and murtajal and determine whether a given word qualifies as mushtarak.

Core Idea
A single word can be coined for multiple meanings from the start; that is mushtarak.

In this passage, the author explains that when a word has multiple meanings from its original coinage, it is called mushtarak (common). He affirms that such words do occur, even for opposite meanings. The term murtajal (coined) is also discussed, with different opinions on whether it falls under mushtarak or manqul. This lesson will help you identify these types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its meanings
Start by noting the word in question and listing its possible meanings. Determine if multiple meanings are present.
2
Check coinage from the beginning
If the word was coined for each of those meanings from the very start, it is mushtarak. The author emphasizes that this occurs even between opposites.
Example: 'qur' can mean both the Quran and the waiting period (idda).
3
Understand the limits of mushtarak
The author clarifies that mushtarak does not imply true generality (لا عموم فيه حقيقة). This means the word does not have a single general meaning covering all instances.
4
Distinguish murtajal
Murtajal is a word coined for a new meaning without prior use. There is a dispute: some consider it a type of mushtarak, others a type of manqul. Recognize this disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَثرَ ، فَإِنْ وُضِعَ لِكُلِّ ابْتِدَاء ، فَمُشْتَرَكُ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ وَاقِعٌ، حَتَّى بَيْنَ الضَّدَّيْنِ ، لَكِنْ لَا عُمُومَ فِيهِ حَقِيقَةٌ ، وَالْمُرْتَجَلُ - قِيلَ ـ مِنَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ ، وَقِيلَ مِنَ الْمَنْقُولِ ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنْ اشْتَهَرَ فِي الثَّانِي فَمَنْقُولُ: شَرْعِيٌّ ، أَوْ عُرْفِيٌّ: خَاصٌ أَوْ عَامٌ. قَالَ سِيبَوَيْهِ : الْأَعْلَامُ كُلُّهَا مَنْقُولَاتٌ خِلَافًا لِلْجُمْهُورِ، وَإِلَّا فَحَقِيقَةٌ وَمَجَاز، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ عَلَاقَةٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَتْ تَشْبِيهَا فَاسْتِعَارَةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَمَجَانٌ مُرْسَلٌ ، وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي أَرْبَعَةٍ وَعِشْرِينَ نَوْعًا. وَلَا يُشْتَرَطُ سَمَاعُ الْجُزْئِيَّاتِ ، نَعَمْ ، يَجِبُ سَمَاعُ أَنْوَاعِهَا ، وَعَلَامَةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ التَّبَادُرُ، وَالْعَرَاءُ عَنِ الْقَرِينَةِ، وَعَلَامَةُ الْمَجَازِ الْإِطْلَاقُ عَلَى الْمُسْتَحِيلِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُ اللَّفْظِ في بَعْضِ الْمَعْنَى ، كَالدَّابَّةِ عَلَى الْحِمَارِ النَّفْلُ وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الاشْتِرَاكِ، وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ النَّقْلِ. وَالْمَجَازُ بِالذَّاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الإِسْمِ، وَأَمَّا الْفِعْلُ وَسَائِرُ الْمُشْتَفَّاتِ وَالْأَدَوَاتُ فَإِنَّمَا يُوجَدُ فِيهَا بِالتَّبعِيَّةِ. وَتَكَثُرُ اللَّفْظِ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْنَى مُرَادَفَةٌ ، وَذَلِكَ وَاقِعٌ؛
And if it is many [meanings], if it is coined for each from the beginning, then it is common (mushtarak), and the truth is that it occurs, even between opposites, but there is no genuine generality in it. And the coined (murtajal) is said to be from mushtarak, and said to be from manqul. Otherwise if it becomes famous in the second [meaning], then it is transferred (manqul): either religious or customary, specific or general. Sibawayh said: all proper nouns are transferred, contrary to the majority. Otherwise [it is] literal (haqiqa) and figurative (majaz), and there must be a relation; if it is resemblance, then it is a metaphor (isti'ara), otherwise it is a free trope (majaz mursal), and they limited it to twenty-four types. Hearing the particulars is not required; rather, it is necessary to hear its types. The sign of the literal is immediate understanding and absence of a context. The sign of the figurative is applying it to the impossible, and using the word for part of the meaning, like 'dabba' for donkey. And the figurative is preferable to commonality, and the figurative is preferable to transfer. And the figurative per se is only in nouns; as for verbs and other derivatives and particles, they occur in it only by following. And the multiplicity of words with one meaning is synonymy (taraduf), and that occurs.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage covers three related topics: first, words with multiple meanings. If they are coined for each meaning independently, they are mushtarak (homonymous), e.g., 'ayn' for eye, spring, etc. The author asserts it occurs, even between opposites. The murtajal (coined for a new meaning without prior use) is disputed: some say it is a type of mushtarak, others of manqul. Second, if a word is transferred from its original meaning to a new, commonly used meaning, it is manqul (transferred), e.g., 'salat' transferred from supplication to the ritual prayer. Sibawayh held that all proper names are manqul. Third, if not transferred, the word is either haqiqa (literal) or majaz (figurative). Haqiqa is indicated by immediate understanding without context; majaz requires a relation (like resemblance for isti'ara, or other relations for majaz mursal). The author notes that majaz is preferred over positing homonymy or transfer. Figurative use primarily applies to nouns; verbs and particles are figurative only by following. Finally, synonymy (taraduf) exists: multiple words for one meaning, e.g., 'asad' and 'layth' for lion.

Key Terms
مشترك
مرتجل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is impossible
B) It occurs
C) It only occurs in poetry
D) It is rare

Key Takeaway
Mushtarak is a word coined for multiple meanings from the start, and it occurs even for opposites, but it does not imply true generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the word 'salat' is mushtarak or not in the context of its original Arabic usage. Explain why.

Manqul (Transferred Words)
Author defines manqul, contrasts religious and customary transfer, and cites Sibawayh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a word as manqul and explain the different categories of transfer.

Core Idea
A word transferred to a new meaning that becomes famous is manqul, and it can be religious or customary.

The author now discusses words that have been transferred from their original meaning to a new one that becomes prevalent. Such words are called manqul (transferred). He distinguishes between religious transfer (manqul shar'i) and customary transfer (manqul 'urfi), which can be specific or general. The renowned grammarian Sibawayh held that all proper names are manqul, contrary to the majority view. This lesson will help you identify and categorize manqul words.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the word was transferred
Check if the word originally had a different meaning and later became famous in a new meaning. If yes, it is manqul.
2
Identify the category of transfer
If the transfer is due to revelation (e.g., 'salat' from supplication to prayer), it is manqul shar'i. If due to common usage (e.g., 'dabba' from any animal to donkey), it is manqul 'urfi.
3
Note Sibawayh's view on proper names
Sibawayh considered all proper names (أعلام) to be manqul, which is a minority opinion. Recognize this as a specific claim within the discussion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَثرَ ، فَإِنْ وُضِعَ لِكُلِّ ابْتِدَاء ، فَمُشْتَرَكُ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ وَاقِعٌ، حَتَّى بَيْنَ الضَّدَّيْنِ ، لَكِنْ لَا عُمُومَ فِيهِ حَقِيقَةٌ ، وَالْمُرْتَجَلُ - قِيلَ ـ مِنَ الْمُشْتَرَكِ ، وَقِيلَ مِنَ الْمَنْقُولِ ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنْ اشْتَهَرَ فِي الثَّانِي فَمَنْقُولُ: شَرْعِيٌّ ، أَوْ عُرْفِيٌّ: خَاصٌ أَوْ عَامٌ. قَالَ سِيبَوَيْهِ : الْأَعْلَامُ كُلُّهَا مَنْقُولَاتٌ خِلَافًا لِلْجُمْهُورِ، وَإِلَّا فَحَقِيقَةٌ وَمَجَاز، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ عَلَاقَةٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَتْ تَشْبِيهَا فَاسْتِعَارَةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَمَجَانٌ مُرْسَلٌ ، وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي أَرْبَعَةٍ وَعِشْرِينَ نَوْعًا. وَلَا يُشْتَرَطُ سَمَاعُ الْجُزْئِيَّاتِ ، نَعَمْ ، يَجِبُ سَمَاعُ أَنْوَاعِهَا ، وَعَلَامَةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ التَّبَادُرُ، وَالْعَرَاءُ عَنِ الْقَرِينَةِ، وَعَلَامَةُ الْمَجَازِ الْإِطْلَاقُ عَلَى الْمُسْتَحِيلِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُ اللَّفْظِ في بَعْضِ الْمَعْنَى ، كَالدَّابَّةِ عَلَى الْحِمَارِ النَّفْلُ وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الاشْتِرَاكِ، وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ النَّقْلِ. وَالْمَجَازُ بِالذَّاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الإِسْمِ، وَأَمَّا الْفِعْلُ وَسَائِرُ الْمُشْتَفَّاتِ وَالْأَدَوَاتُ فَإِنَّمَا يُوجَدُ فِيهَا بِالتَّبعِيَّةِ. وَتَكَثُرُ اللَّفْظِ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْنَى مُرَادَفَةٌ ، وَذَلِكَ وَاقِعٌ؛
And if it is many [meanings], if it is coined for each from the beginning, then it is common (mushtarak), and the truth is that it occurs, even between opposites, but there is no genuine generality in it. And the coined (murtajal) is said to be from mushtarak, and said to be from manqul. Otherwise if it becomes famous in the second [meaning], then it is transferred (manqul): either religious or customary, specific or general. Sibawayh said: all proper nouns are transferred, contrary to the majority. Otherwise [it is] literal (haqiqa) and figurative (majaz), and there must be a relation; if it is resemblance, then it is a metaphor (isti'ara), otherwise it is a free trope (majaz mursal), and they limited it to twenty-four types. Hearing the particulars is not required; rather, it is necessary to hear its types. The sign of the literal is immediate understanding and absence of a context. The sign of the figurative is applying it to the impossible, and using the word for part of the meaning, like 'dabba' for donkey. And the figurative is preferable to commonality, and the figurative is preferable to transfer. And the figurative per se is only in nouns; as for verbs and other derivatives and particles, they occur in it only by following. And the multiplicity of words with one meaning is synonymy (taraduf), and that occurs.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage covers three related topics: first, words with multiple meanings. If they are coined for each meaning independently, they are mushtarak (homonymous), e.g., 'ayn' for eye, spring, etc. The author asserts it occurs, even between opposites. The murtajal (coined for a new meaning without prior use) is disputed: some say it is a type of mushtarak, others of manqul. Second, if a word is transferred from its original meaning to a new, commonly used meaning, it is manqul (transferred), e.g., 'salat' transferred from supplication to the ritual prayer. Sibawayh held that all proper names are manqul. Third, if not transferred, the word is either haqiqa (literal) or majaz (figurative). Haqiqa is indicated by immediate understanding without context; majaz requires a relation (like resemblance for isti'ara, or other relations for majaz mursal). The author notes that majaz is preferred over positing homonymy or transfer. Figurative use primarily applies to nouns; verbs and particles are figurative only by following. Finally, synonymy (taraduf) exists: multiple words for one meaning, e.g., 'asad' and 'layth' for lion.

Key Terms
منقول
شرعي
عرفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Sibawayh claim about all proper nouns?
A) They are mushtarak
B) They are manqul
C) They are haqiqa
D) They are majaz

Key Takeaway
Manqul words are those transferred to a new prevalent meaning; proper names are considered manqul by Sibawayh.

Haqiqa, Majaz, and Taraduf
Author distinguishes haqiqa and majaz, explains signs, and notes that majaz occurs primarily in nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqa and majaz, identify their signs, and recognize the phenomenon of synonymy (taraduf).

Core Idea
Literal meaning is understood immediately without context; figurative requires a relation and is preferred over homonymy.

The author now turns to the distinction between haqiqa (literal) and majaz (figurative) words. Haqiqa is the original usage without any contextual cue, while majaz requires a relation (علاقة) between the literal and figurative meanings. He also discusses the types of majaz: isti'ara (metaphor) based on resemblance, and majaz mursal (free trope) based on other relations. Additionally, the concept of taraduf (synonymy) is introduced: multiple words for the same meaning. This lesson will equip you with the tools to classify words as literal or figurative, and to identify synonymy.

Guided Path
1
Determine if the word is used in its original meaning
If the word is used in its original coinage without any contextual cue (قرينة), it is haqiqa (literal).
2
Check for a relation in non-literal use
If the word is used in a meaning other than its original, look for a relation (علاقة). If there is a relation, it is majaz (figurative).
3
Identify the type of majaz
If the relation is resemblance (تشبيه), it is isti'ara (metaphor). Otherwise, it is majaz mursal (free trope). The author mentions that scholars have identified 24 types of majaz mursal.
Example: 'dabba' used for donkey is majaz mursal (relation of part to whole or specific to general?).
4
Apply the signs
For haqiqa: immediate understanding (تبادر) and absence of context. For majaz: using the word for something impossible (إطلاق على المستحيل) or using it for part of the meaning (استعمال في بعض المعنى).
5
Understand the preference for majaz
When a word has multiple possible interpretations, interpreting it as majaz is preferable to assuming it is mushtarak or manqul. This is a principle in usul al-fiqh.

Real vs. Verbal Definitions (Haqiqi and Lafzi)
Author defines mua'rif and divides it into haqiqi and lafzi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (haqiqi) and a verbal definition (lafzi) and explain the conditions under which each is used.

Core Idea
Real definition gives new knowledge; verbal definition clarifies a term.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational concept of mua'rif (definition) and divides it into two types: real and verbal. Understanding this distinction is critical for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences, because a real definition reveals the essence of a thing, while a verbal definition merely explains what a name means. As you work through the lesson, you will learn not only what each type is, but also how to identify which type is being used when you encounter a definition in a usul or kalam text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines the term 'mua'rif' (definition). Notice that it is defined as something predicated of a thing to give a conceptualization (taswir).
This is a meta-definition: defining what a definition is.
2
Understand the two methods of conceptualization
The author says conceptualization is achieved either by 'tahsil' (acquisition) or 'tafsir' (explanation). Tahsil brings a new mental form into being; tafsir clarifies an already present concept.
3
Distinguish haqiqi and lafzi definitions
A real definition (haqiqi) is the one that involves tahsil – acquiring a new form. A verbal definition (lafzi) is the one that involves tafsir – explaining an existing form.
The Arabic words for acquisition and explanation are the key indicators.
4
Apply the condition of known existence
The passage adds: if the existence of the defined thing is known, the real definition gives the essence according to reality; if not, it only gives the meaning of the name. This helps determine whether a definition is truly real or merely nominal.
This condition prevents mistaking a verbal definition for a real one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ تَصْوِيرًا ، تَخْصِيلًا أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا، وَالثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ ، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْمِ.
The definition (mua'rif) of a thing is what is predicated of it to give a concept, whether to acquire (a new form) or to explain (an existing form). The second is verbal (lafzi), and the first is real (haqiqi). In the real definition, there is the acquisition of a form that was not present; if its existence is known, it is according to reality, otherwise according to the name.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement about 'mua'rif' (definition). The author defines mua'rif as something that is predicated of a thing in order to provide a conceptualization (taswir). This is done either to acquire a new mental form (tahsil) or to explain an existing one (tafsir). The first is called haqiqi (real definition) and the second lafzi (verbal definition). A real definition actually produces a new concept in the mind. If we already know that the defined thing exists, the definition gives us the true essence; if we do not know its existence, it only gives us the meaning of the name (ism). This is a foundational distinction in logic for understanding the purpose and scope of definitions.

Key Terms
مُعَرِّف
حَقِيقِي
لَفْظِي
تَصْوِير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The real definition uses synonyms; the verbal definition uses technical terms.
C) The real definition is always longer; the verbal definition is shorter.
D) The real definition is only for concrete objects; the verbal definition is for abstract terms.

Key Takeaway
A definition (mua'rif) is either real (haqiqi), giving a new concept, or verbal (lafzi), explaining an existing one, based on whether the existence of the defined is known.

Definition and Its Conditions
Definition of definition and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main conditions of a valid definition and apply them to assess whether an example definition from Islamic sciences is valid.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be both clearer than and coextensive with the definiendum.

In this passage, the author explains what a definition (mu'arif) is and lays out the essential conditions it must meet. These conditions are not mere academic formalities—they are critical tools for precise reasoning in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam. Understanding them will help you evaluate definitions used by scholars and avoid common mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiens and definiendum
The passage begins by defining 'definition' itself. The definiens (mu'arif) is 'that which is predicated of a thing conceptually' — note the purpose: taswir (conceptualization). The definiendum is the thing being defined.
2
Check the condition of clarity (ajla)
The author states that the definiens must be clearer than the definiendum. If it is equally unknown or less clear, the definition fails. For example, defining 'salah' as 'a specific form of worship with conditions' is valid only if the terms used are clearer than 'salah' itself.
3
Check the condition of coextensiveness (musawat)
The definiens must apply to all and only instances of the definiendum (ittarad and in'ikas). A definition that is broader ('aam) or narrower (akhass) is invalid. For instance, defining 'sawm' as 'abstention from food' is broader (includes dieting), so it fails.
4
Evaluate definition by example
The author allows definition by example (ta'rif bil-mithal) as a form of specific resemblance, but the correct view is that it is permissible by the more general. This is a nuanced condition to be aware of.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ تَصْوِيرًا ، تَخْصِيلًا أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا، وَالثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ ، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْمِ.
وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى ، فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةٌ وَجَهَالَةٌ ، وَلاَ بِالْأَخْفَى، وَلا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا ، فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ وَالاِنْعِكَاسُ، فَلَا يَصحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصّ . وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمِّ.
وَهُوَ حَدٌ ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُمَيِّرُ ذَاتِيًّا، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ رَسُمٌ، وَهُوَ تَامٌ إِنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ الْقَرِيبِ ، وَإِلَّا فَنَاقِصٌ ، فَالْحَدُّ النَّامُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ وَالْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبَيْنِ، وَهُوَ الْمُوصِلُ إِلَى الْكُنْهِ. وَيُسْتَحْسَنُ تَقْدِيمُ الْجِنْسِ، وَيَجِبُ تَقْيِيدُ أَحَدِهِمَا بِالْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَقْبَلُ الزِّيَادَةَ وَالنُّقْصَانَ.
وَالْبَسِيطُ لَا يُحَدُّ، وَقَدْ يُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَالْمُرَكَّبُ يُحَدُّ وَيُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَقَدْ لَا يُحَدُّ به . وَالتَّحْدِيدُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا ؛ فَإِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مُشْتَبِةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ الْعَامِّ، وَالْفَصْلَ بِالْخَاصَّةِ، وَالْفَرْقُ مِنَ الْغَوَامِضِ.
The definition of a thing is what is predicated of it conceptually, as a realization or an explanation. The second is the verbal, and the first is the real. In it is the realization of a form not yet obtained. If its existence is known, it is according to reality; otherwise, according to the name. It is necessary that the definiens be clearer [than the definiendum]. So a definition by what is equal [to it] while one is ignorant is not valid, nor by what is less clear. And it must be coextensive, so coextensiveness and conversion must obtain. So definition by the more general or the more specific is invalid. Definition by example is definition by specific resemblance, and the correct opinion is that it is permissible by the more general. And it is a hadd (essential definition) if the distinguisher is essential; otherwise it is a rasm (accidental definition). And it is complete (tam) if it includes the proximate genus; otherwise it is incomplete (naqis). The perfect (taam) hadd is what includes the proximate genus and the proximate differentia, and it leads to the quiddity (kunh). It is recommended to present the genus first, and it is necessary to restrict one by the other, and it does not admit increase or decrease. The simple thing cannot be defined, but it may be used to define; the composite thing can be defined and can be used to define, and sometimes it is not used to define. Real definition is very difficult, for the genus is confounded with the common accident, and the differentia with the property, and the distinction is among the subtle matters.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is definitional, explaining the nature and conditions of definition (ta'rif) and its types. The author begins by defining 'definition' (mu'arif) as that which is predicated of a thing conceptually, serving either to realize a concept (tasawwur) or to explain it. The second type (explanatory) is verbal, the first (conceptual) is real. The real definition produces a form not previously had. If the existence of the thing is known, it is a definition of reality; otherwise, it is a definition of the name. The conditions follow: the definiens must be clearer (ajla) than the definiendum—definition by something equally unknown is invalid, as is definition by something less clear. It must also be coextensive (musawi), meaning it applies to all and only the same things (ittarad and in'ikas). Therefore, definition by a broader or narrower term is invalid. Definition by example is ruled as permissible by the more general, though it is a specific resemblance. Next, the author classifies definitions: if the distinguishing factor is essential (dhati), it is a hadd (essential definition); otherwise, it is a rasm (accidental description). A hadd is complete (tam) if it includes the proximate genus and proximate differentia; otherwise it is incomplete. The perfect (taam) hadd includes both and reveals the quiddity (kunh). It is recommended to state the genus first, and the differentia must restrict the genus. A hadd does not admit increase or decrease. Regarding what can be defined: simple things (e.g., non-composite quiddities) cannot be defined (since they have no genus and differentia), but they can be used to define composite things. Composite things can be defined and can be used to define other things, though sometimes not. The author concludes that real definition is very difficult because genus is easily confused with common accident, and differentia with property (special accident), and distinguishing them is a subtle matter.

Key Terms
معرف
أجلى
مساو
اطراد
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is explicitly invalid as a definition?
A) Definition by what is equally unknown
B) Definition by what is clearer
C) Definition by the more general
D) Definition by example

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be both clearer than (ajla) and coextensive with (musawi) the definiendum, otherwise it fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the definition: 'Salah is worship consisting of specific actions and utterances.' Check whether it satisfies the condition of coextensiveness: Does it apply to all forms of salah (including obligatory and supererogatory)? Does it exclude non-salah acts like dua?
?
Identify whether 'prayer is an act of worship' is a valid definition according to the passage. Explain why or why not.

Hadd and Rasm

Hadd and Rasm: Essential and Accidental Definition
Classification of definitions into hadd and rasm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definition as either a hadd (essential) or a rasm (accidental) and identify the components of a complete hadd.

Core Idea
A definition is a hadd if it uses essential properties, and a rasm if it uses accidental properties.

Having established the general conditions of definition, the author now classifies definitions into two main types: hadd (essential definition) and rasm (accidental description). This classification is crucial for understanding how scholars define key concepts in Islamic sciences. The passage also explains what makes a definition complete or incomplete, and which things can be defined.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish hadd from rasm
The author says: if the distinguisher (mumayyiz) in the definition is essential (dhati), it is a hadd; if it is accidental, it is a rasm. For example, 'human is a rational animal' uses essential properties (animal: genus; rational: differentia) so it is a hadd. 'Human is a laughing animal' uses an accident (laughter), so it is a rasm.
2
Identify the components of a complete hadd
A perfect (taam) hadd includes the proximate genus (jins qarib) and the proximate differentia (fasl qarib). It must be presented with the genus first, and the differentia must restrict the genus. The definition does not admit increase or decrease.
3
Recognize incomplete definitions
A rasm is complete (tam) if it includes the proximate genus, otherwise it is incomplete (naqis). Similarly, a hadd that lacks either the proximate genus or differentia is incomplete.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the definition of 'qiyas' in usul al-fiqh: 'extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause ('illah).' If this uses essential components of qiyas (asl, far', hukm, 'illah), it is a hadd. If it merely describes it as 'a method of reasoning,' it may be a rasm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُعَرِّفُ الشَّيْءِ مَا يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهِ تَصْوِيرًا ، تَخْصِيلًا أَوْ تَفْسِيرًا، وَالثَّانِي اللَّفْظِيُّ، وَالْأَوَّلُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ ، فَفِيهِ تَحْصِيلُ صُورَةٍ غَيْرِ حَاصِلَةٍ ، فَإِنْ عُلِمَ وُجُودُهَا فَهُوَ بِحَسَبِ الْحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِلَّا فَبِحَسَبِ الإِسْمِ.
وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى ، فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةٌ وَجَهَالَةٌ ، وَلاَ بِالْأَخْفَى، وَلا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا ، فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ وَالاِنْعِكَاسُ، فَلَا يَصحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصّ . وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمِّ.
وَهُوَ حَدٌ ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُمَيِّرُ ذَاتِيًّا، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ رَسُمٌ، وَهُوَ تَامٌ إِنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ الْقَرِيبِ ، وَإِلَّا فَنَاقِصٌ ، فَالْحَدُّ النَّامُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ وَالْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبَيْنِ، وَهُوَ الْمُوصِلُ إِلَى الْكُنْهِ. وَيُسْتَحْسَنُ تَقْدِيمُ الْجِنْسِ، وَيَجِبُ تَقْيِيدُ أَحَدِهِمَا بِالْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَقْبَلُ الزِّيَادَةَ وَالنُّقْصَانَ.
وَالْبَسِيطُ لَا يُحَدُّ، وَقَدْ يُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَالْمُرَكَّبُ يُحَدُّ وَيُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَقَدْ لَا يُحَدُّ به . وَالتَّحْدِيدُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا ؛ فَإِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مُشْتَبِةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ الْعَامِّ، وَالْفَصْلَ بِالْخَاصَّةِ، وَالْفَرْقُ مِنَ الْغَوَامِضِ.
The definition of a thing is what is predicated of it conceptually, as a realization or an explanation. The second is the verbal, and the first is the real. In it is the realization of a form not yet obtained. If its existence is known, it is according to reality; otherwise, according to the name. The Genus and Differentia: A Unified Concept
Author explains how the mind combines genus and differentia into a unified definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the genus and differentia combine in a definition, distinguishing between external addition and internal restriction.

Core Idea
The genus is not mixed from outside; it is determined from within.

This passage delves into the subtle relationship between genus and differentia in forming a definition. The author clarifies that the mind does not simply add the differentia as an external label; rather, it restricts the indeterminate genus, actualizing it into a unified concept. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how definitions work in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms 'جنس' (genus), 'مبهم' (indeterminate), 'تحصل' (actualization), and 'حد' (definition). Notice how they are used to describe the process.
2
Extract the author's core claim
The author states that the differentia is added 'لَا عَلَى أَنَّهُ مَعْنَى خَارِجٌ لَاحِقٌ بِهِ' (not as an external meaning attaching to it) but as a restriction that actualizes the genus. Paraphrase this in your own words.
3
Analyze the metaphor of scattered pearls
The author compares the components of a definition to scattered pearls—each distinct in a respect. Consider why this metaphor is used: it illustrates the mental multiplicity before unification.
4
Contrast with a potential misunderstanding
Some might think genus and differentia are separate ingredients. The author argues the differentia is included within the genus ('مُتَضَمِّنًا فِيهِ'). Explain why this inclusion is crucial for the definition to correspond to the defined thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ هَهُنَا مَبَاحِثُ ،
الْأَوَّلُ : الْجِنْسُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مُبْهَما ، لَكِنَّ الذَّهْنَ قَدْ يَخْلُقُ لَهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ التَعَقُلُ وُجُودًا مُنْفَرِدًا ، وَأَضَافَ إِلَيْهِ زِيَادَةٌ، لَا عَلَى أَنَّهُ مَعْنَى خَارِجٌ لَاحِقٌ بِهِ، بَلْ قَيَّدَهُ لِأَجَل تَحَصُّلِهِ وَتَعَيْنِهِ ، مُتَضَمِّنًا فِيهِ، فَإِذَا صَارَ مُحَصَّلًا لَمْ يَكُنْ شَيْئًا آخَرَ فَإِنَّ التَّحَصلَ لَيْسَ يُغيّره ، بَلْ يُحَقِّقه. ؛
فَإِذَا نَظَرْتَ إِلَى الْحَدِّ وَجَدْتَهُ مُؤَلَّفَا مِنْ عِدَّةِ مَعَانٍ، كُلٌّ مِنْهَا مُغَايِرٌ، كَالدُّرَرِ الْمَنْثُورَةِ غَيْرُ الْآخَرِ بِنَحْوٍ مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارِ ، فَهُنَاكَ كَفْرَةٌ بِالْفِعْلُ، فَلَا يُحْمَلُ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى الْآخَرَ، وَلَا عَلَى الْمَجْمُوعِ، وَلَيْسَ مَعْنَى الْحَدِّ بِهَذَا الِاعْتِبَارِ مَعْنَى الْمَحْدُودِ الْمَعْقُولِ ، لَكِنْ إِذَا لُوحِظَ إِلَى إِبْهَام أَحَدِهِمَا وَقُيدَ بِالْآخَرِ مُتَضَمَّنا فيه
Then here are discussions: The first: the genus. Although it is indeterminate, the mind creates for it, in terms of intellection, an isolated existence, and adds to it an addition—not that it is an external meaning that attaches to it, but it restricts it for the sake of its actualization and determination, being included in it. So when it becomes actualized, it is not something else, for actualization does not change it but rather realizes it. So if you look at the definition, you find it composed of several meanings, each different, like scattered pearls—each is other than the other in some respect. So there is a multiplicity in actuality; one of them is not predicated of the other, nor of the aggregate. And the meaning of the definition in this respect is not the meaning of the defined as understood, but when one considers the indeterminacy of one of them and it is restricted by the other, being included in it.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion, specifically a sub-discussion (mabahith) on the nature of the genus in definition. The author addresses a subtle point: the genus is inherently indeterminate (mubham), but the mind can conceive it as having an isolated existence through intellection. The mind then adds an 'addition' (the differentia), not as an external meaning attaching to the genus, but as a restriction that actualizes and determines the genus. The genus is included in the species, so when the species is actualized, it is not a separate thing; the actualization does not change the genus but realizes it. To illustrate, the author compares the definition to scattered pearls—multiple distinct meanings in the mind, each different in some respect. There is an actual multiplicity; one component is not predicated of the other, nor of the whole aggregate. The meaning of the definition in this respect (considering the components as separate in the mind) is not identical to the meaning of the defined thing as understood. However, when one considers the indeterminacy of the genus and it is restricted by the differentia (being included within it), then the definition corresponds to the defined. This clarifies that the genus and differentia are mentally distinct but are combined in a unified concept that corresponds to the real thing.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): The genus as an indeterminate common concept that becomes determined by the differentia to form the species. [Note: No significant shift; this is a standard discussion on the relation of genus to species.]
مبهم (mubham): Indeterminate: the genus in itself is vague until the differentia restricts it. [Note: Common term in logic for the indeterminate nature of genus.]
تحصل (tahassul): Actualization: the process by which the genus becomes determinate through the addition of the differentia. [Note: Stable term used in discussions of definition and quiddity.]
حد (hadd): Definition: the composite of genus and differentia; considered as a unified concept that corresponds to the defined. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the genus and the differentia when they form a definition?
A) They are separate entities added together externally
B) The differentia restricts the genus and is included in it
C) The genus contains the differentia as part of it
D) They are identical in meaning

Key Takeaway
The differentia does not add an external meaning to the genus; it restricts the genus internally, actualizing it into a unified definition that corresponds to the defined thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the definition of 'sawm' (fasting) from usul al-fiqh: 'Sawm is abstention from certain designated things with intention from dawn to sunset, accompanied by specific rules.' Identify the genus (abstention) and the differentia (the detailed restrictions). Explain how the differentia restricts the genus in the sense described in the passage, rather than being an external addition.
?
2. Reflect on the metaphor of scattered pearls. Why does the author say that, from one perspective, the components of a definition are like scattered pearls? How does this perspective differ from the unified perspective? Provide your answer using an example from Islamic sciences.

The Clarity Condition in Definition
Author states the first condition: definer must be clearer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a definition violates the clarity condition by being synonymous or obscure.

Core Idea
A definition must make things clearer, not introduce more confusion.

In this passage, the author lays down the first condition for a valid definition: the definer must be clearer than the definiendum. This rules out definitions using synonyms or terms that are even more obscure. Let's break down why this condition is essential for any sound definition in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definer
Look at the definition: find the term being defined (definiendum) and the term(s) used to define it (definer). The author says the definer must be 'ajla' (clearer). Check if the definer is indeed clearer.
2
Test against synonym definitions
If the definer is a synonym (musawi), it does not increase clarity; it just repeats. The author says this is like 'knowledge and ignorance' — you don't gain new understanding. Test an example: defining 'fiqh' as 'understanding' is not a valid definition.
3
Test against obscure definitions
If the definer is more obscure (akhfa) than the definiendum, it violates the clarity condition. For example, defining 'wudu' using rare technical terms that the average person does not know. The author prohibits this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى ، فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةٌ وَجَهَالَةٌ ، وَلاَ بِالْأَخْفَى، وَلا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا ، فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ وَالاِنْعِكَاسُ، فَلَا يَصحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصّ . وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمِّ.
And it is necessary that the definer be clearer, so definition by the equal is not valid (a case of knowledge and ignorance), nor by the more obscure, and it is necessary that it be coextensive, so coextensiveness and converse must hold, so definition by the wider or narrower is not valid. And definition by example is definition by specific similarity, and the correct view is that it is permissible by the wider.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif) in logic. The author begins by stating necessary conditions: the definer (mu'arrij) must be clearer (ajla) than the definiendum. This rules out definition by a synonym (musawi) because that would involve ignorance (jahala) and definition by something more obscure (akhfa). Then he states that the definer must be coextensive (musawi) with the definiendum, meaning it must apply to exactly the same things (mutarid wa munkas). This prohibits definition by a wider term (a'am) or a narrower term (akhass). Finally, he discusses definition by example (tamthil) as definition by specific similarity, and the correct position is that it is permissible if done by a wider term.

A valid definition must use a definer that is clearer than the definiendum, ruling out synonyms and obscure terms.

The Coextensiveness Condition: Al-Ittirād wa al-In'ikās
Author states the condition of coextensiveness and prohibition of wider/narrower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a definition is coextensive and identify if it is wider or narrower than the definiendum.

Core Idea
A definition must match exactly the set of things it defines — no more, no less.

After establishing clarity, the author introduces the second critical condition: coextensiveness (musāwi). The definer must apply to all and only the instances of the definiendum. This is expressed through two properties: al-ittirād (coextensiveness in the positive sense) and al-in'ikās (converse, i.e., the definiendum applies to all instances of the definer). Any deviation makes the definition invalid. Let's see how to apply this condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its extension
Determine the set of individuals or concepts that the definiendum covers. For example, 'salah' covers all specific prayers with specific conditions.
2
Identify the extension of the definer
Determine the set covered by the definer. For instance, if the definition is 'salah is worship', the definer 'worship' covers all acts of worship, not just salah.
3
Check coextensiveness: ittirād and in'ikās
Check that every instance of the definiendum is covered by the definer (ittirād) and that every instance of the definer is an instance of the definiendum (in'ikās). If either fails, the definition is either wider or narrower and thus invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْمُعَرِّفُ أَجْلَى ، فَلَا يَصِحُ بِالْمُسَاوِي مَعْرِفَةٌ وَجَهَالَةٌ ، وَلاَ بِالْأَخْفَى، وَلا بُدَّ أَنْ يَكُونَ مُسَاوِيًا ، فَيَجِبُ الاِطَّرَادُ وَالاِنْعِكَاسُ، فَلَا يَصحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِالْأَعَمِّ وَالْأَخَصّ . وَالتَّعْرِيفُ بِالْمِثَالِ تَعْرِيفٌ بِالْمُشَابَهَةِ الْمُخْتَصَّةِ، وَالْحَقُّ جَوَازُهُ بِالْأَعَمِّ.
And it is necessary that the definer be clearer, so definition by the equal is not valid (a case of knowledge and ignorance), nor by the more obscure, and it is necessary that it be coextensive, so coextensiveness and converse must hold, so definition by the wider or narrower is not valid. And definition by example is definition by specific similarity, and the correct view is that it is permissible by the wider.
Source pages p. 123

This passage defines the conditions for a valid definition (ta'rif) in logic. The author begins by stating necessary conditions: the definer (mu'arrij) must be clearer (ajla) than the definiendum. This rules out definition by a synonym (musawi) because that would involve ignorance (jahala) and definition by something more obscure (akhfa). Then he states that the definer must be coextensive (musawi) with the definiendum, meaning it must apply to exactly the same things (mutarid wa munkas). This prohibits definition by a wider term (a'am) or a narrower term (akhass). Finally, he discusses definition by example (tamthil) as definition by specific similarity, and the correct position is that it is permissible if done by a wider term.

Definition by Example: Its Permissibility and Limits
Author discusses definition by example and its permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when definition by example is permissible and when it is not.

Core Idea
Definition by example can be a valid tool, but only when it uses a specific similarity under a wider category.

The passage ends with a discussion of definition by example (tamthīl). The author clarifies that this type of definition is based on specific similarity (mushābaha mukhtaṣṣa) and that the correct position allows it when done by the wider (a'am). This means giving an example that is a particular instance of a broader concept can help clarify the meaning, provided the example is not misleading.

Guided Path
1
Understand what definition by example is
Definition by example means explaining a term by giving an instance or example. For instance, to define 'wājib' one might say 'like the five daily prayers'.
2
Recognize the condition: specific similarity
The example must share a specific similarity with the definiendum that makes it representative. It cannot be a random or tangential similarity.
3
Apply the permissibility by the wider
The author says the correct view is that such definition is permissible 'by the wider'. This means the example should be a specific instance of the broader category, so the broader category is the definer. For example, defining 'salāt' by giving an example of a specific prayer (like Fajr) is permissible because Fajr is a type of salāt.

Hadd vs Rasm: Essential and Descriptive Definitions
Author distinguishes between hadd and rasm based on the differentia, and between complete and incomplete definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a hadd and a rasm, and identify whether a given definition is complete or incomplete based on the inclusion of the proximate genus.

Core Idea
A definition is a hadd if its distinguisher is essential; otherwise it is a rasm.

In Islamic logic, definitions are classified into two fundamental types: hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition). The critical distinction lies in whether the characteristic that distinguishes the defined thing from others is essential (dhati) or merely accidental. Additionally, each type can be complete (tam) or incomplete (naqis) depending on whether it includes the proximate genus. Mastering this classification is essential for precise definition in all Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Look at what is being defined. In the passage, the author is explaining the criteria for classifying definitions themselves.
2
Find the distinguisher (mumayyiz)
Determine whether the characteristic used to separate the definiendum from others is essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi). If essential, it is a hadd; if accidental, it is a rasm.
The passage states: 'it is a hadd if the distinguisher is essential, otherwise it is a rasm'.
3
Check for proximate genus
See if the definition includes the proximate genus (al-jins al-qarib). If it does, the definition is complete (tam); otherwise, it is incomplete (naqis).
The passage says: 'it is complete if it includes the proximate genus, otherwise it is incomplete'.
4
Apply to an example
Take the definition 'Man is a rational animal'. The genus 'animal' is proximate, and 'rational' is an essential differentia. Thus it is a complete hadd.
This example is from kalam; the hadd leads to the essence (kunh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهُوَ حَدٌ ، إِنْ كَانَ الْمُمَيِّرُ ذَاتِيًّا، وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ رَسُمٌ، وَهُوَ تَامٌ إِنِ اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ الْقَرِيبِ ، وَإِلَّا فَنَاقِصٌ ، فَالْحَدُّ النَّامُ مَا اشْتَمَلَ عَلَى الْجِنْسِ وَالْفَصْلِ الْقَرِيبَيْنِ، وَهُوَ الْمُوصِلُ إِلَى الْكُنْهِ. وَيُسْتَحْسَنُ تَقْدِيمُ الْجِنْسِ، وَيَجِبُ تَقْيِيدُ أَحَدِهِمَا بِالْآخَرِ، وَهُوَ لَا يَقْبَلُ الزِّيَادَةَ وَالنُّقْصَانَ.
And it is a hadd if the distinguisher is essential, otherwise it is a rasm. And it is complete if it includes the proximate genus, otherwise it is incomplete. So the complete hadd is what includes the proximate genus and differentia, and it is what leads to the essence. It is recommended to present the genus, and it is necessary to qualify one by the other, and it does not accept increase or decrease.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage classifies definitions into two main types: hadd (essential definition) and rasm (description). If the distinguisher (mumayyiz) is essential (dhati), it is a hadd; otherwise it is a rasm. Then it classifies each into complete (tam) and incomplete (naqis) based on whether it includes the proximate genus. The complete hadd must contain both the proximate genus and the proximate differentia, and it leads to knowledge of the essence (kunh). The author notes that it is recommended to mention the genus first, and that the parts must be interdefined (one qualified by the other). The hadd is precise and allows no addition or subtraction.

Key Terms
الحد
الرسم
الجنس القريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It includes the proximate genus
B) It includes the differentia

D) It is complete

Key Takeaway
A complete hadd includes both the proximate genus and an essential differentia and leads to the essence of the thing defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simple, Composite, and the Difficulty of Real Definition
Author discusses definability of simple/composite and difficulty of real definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between simple and composite entities based on their definability, and explain why real definition is difficult due to confusion between essential and accidental attributes.

Core Idea
Simple entities cannot be defined; only composites can, but real definition is elusive.

In this passage, the author introduces a foundational distinction in logic: simple entities (without parts) cannot be defined, while composite entities (with parts) can. However, even when definition is possible, achieving a real definition (al-tahdid al-haqiqi) is extremely difficult because the genus can be mistaken for a common accident, and the differentia for a proper accident. This lesson will help you navigate these subtleties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of entities
The author distinguishes between al-basīṭ (simple) and al-murakkab (composite). Note the key property: simple has no parts, composite has parts.
2
Understand their definability
Simple is NOT defined (لا يُحَدّ), but it CAN be used to define (يُحَدّ به). Composite IS defined (يُحَدّ) and CAN be used to define, but sometimes is not used to define (قَدْ لا يُحَدّ به). Clarify: 'defined' here means having a real definition (hadd haqiqi) by genus and differentia.
3
Analyze the difficulty of real definition
The author identifies two confusions: genus is confused with common accident (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm), and differentia with proper accident (al-khāṣṣa). The distinction between these is subtle (min al-ghawāmiḍ).
4
Apply the distinction to a sample term
Choose a term like 'human'. The genus is 'animal', but 'animal' could be mistaken for a common accident like 'moving'. The differentia 'rational' could be mistaken for a proper accident like 'laughing'. Recognize why a real definition is hard to ascertain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْبَسِيطُ لَا يُحَدُّ، وَقَدْ يُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَالْمُرَكَّبُ يُحَدُّ وَيُحَدُّ بِهِ، وَقَدْ لَا يُحَدُّ به . وَالتَّحْدِيدُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا ; فَإِنَّ الْجِنْسَ مُشْتَبِةٌ بِالْعَرَضِ الْعَامِّ، وَالْفَصْلَ بالْخَاصَّةِ، وَالْفَرْقُ مِنَ الْغَوَامِضِ.
And the simple is not defined, and it may be used to define; and the composite is defined and may be used to define, and sometimes it is not used to define. And real definition is very difficult, for the genus is confused with the common accident, and the differentia with the proper accident, and the distinction is among the subtle matters.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage addresses the definability of simple (basit) and composite (murakkab) entities. A simple entity (one with no parts) cannot be defined (because definition requires genus and differentia, implying composition), but it can be used as a definer (e.g., in definitions of composites). A composite entity can be defined (since it has parts) and can also be used as a definer, but sometimes it is not used as a definer (e.g., if it is not appropriate). Then the author remarks on the difficulty of real definition (al-tahdid al-haqiqi): the genus is easily confused with a common accident (al-'arad al-'amm), the differentia with a proper accident (al-khassa), and the distinction is among the subtle issues (al-ghawamid).

Key Terms
البسيط
المركب
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because composite entities are rarely defined.
D) Because the author did not provide examples.

Key Takeaway
Simple entities cannot be defined by genus and differentia, and even for composite entities, real definition is challenging due to subtle confusion between essential and accidental attributes.

How the Mind Constructs a Unified Concept from Definition Parts
Author explains how the mind unifies definition parts via restrictive composition, refuting al-Razi's doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how restrictive composition (tarkīb taqyīdī) resolves al-Razi's doubt about definition and apply it to an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
Definition parts are not merely listed; the mind restricts the genus to yield a single unified concept.

This passage tackles a foundational puzzle: if a definition lists multiple parts, how can it give us a single concept of the thing defined? The author introduces a distinction between two kinds of mental composition—predictive (which involves judgment) and restrictive (which merely depicts unity). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how definitions work in Islamic logic, and it directly answers al-Razi's famous objection that definition is either redundant or impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem: al-Razi's doubt
Al-Razi argued that definition is either by the essence itself (redundant), by all its parts (same), or by accidents (cannot reveal essence). Therefore all definitions are false. Read the passage to see how the author states this doubt.
The doubt is at the end of the passage.
2
Distinguish two types of composition
The passage contrasts predicative composition (tarkīb khabarī) with restrictive composition (tarkīb taqyīdī). Predicative composition includes a judgment (like 'Man is rational'), while restrictive composition simply restricts one concept by another without judgment. Find where the author explains this.
Look for 'إِلَّا أَنَّ هُنَاكَ تَرْكِيباً خَبَرِيَّا' and 'وَهُنَا تَرْكِيبٌ تَقْيِيدِيٌّ'.
3
Observe how the mind unifies parts
The author says that when the ambiguous genus is restricted by the differentia (e.g., 'animal' restricted by 'rational'), the mind does not keep them separate but creates a single concept. The differentia is not an external addition but an internal restriction that actualizes the genus. Trace the argument with the example 'rational animal' for 'man'.
The key phrase: 'يَخْلُقُ لَهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعَقُّلُ وُجُودًا مُنْفَرِدًا'.
4
Apply the resolution to al-Razi's doubt
The author concludes that the definition is not a mere listing of parts; rather, the parts are unified in the mind through restrictive composition. Thus definition is not obtaining the already obtained—it yields a new unified conceptualization (taṣawwur). Confirm how this refutes each horn of al-Razi's trilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ هَهُنَا مَبَاحِثُ ، الْأَوَّلُ : الْجِنْسُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مُبْهَماً ، لَكِنَّ الذَّهْنَ قَدْ يَخْلُقُ لَهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ التَّعَقُّلُ وُجُودًا مُنْفَرِدًا ، وَأَضَافَ إِلَيْهِ زِيَادَةٌ، لَا عَلَى أَنَّهُ مَعْنَى خَارِجٌ لَاحِقٌ بِهِ، بَلْ قَيَّدَهُ لِأَجَلِ تَحَصُّلِهِ وَتَعَيُّنِهِ ، مُتَضَمِّنًا فِيهِ، فَإِذَا صَارَ مُحَصَّلاً لَمْ يَكُنْ شَيْئًا آخَرَ فَإِنَّ التَّحَصُّلَ لَيْسَ يُغَيِّرُهُ ، بَلْ يُحَقِّقُهُ. فَإِذَا نَظَرْتَ إِلَى الْحَدِّ وَجَدْتَهُ مُؤَلَّفاً مِنْ عِدَّةِ مَعَانٍ، كُلٌّ مِنْهَا مُغَايِرٌ لِلْآخَرِ بِنَحْوٍ مِنَ الِاعْتِبَارِ ، فَهُنَاكَ كَثْرَةٌ بِالْفِعْلِ، فَلَا يُحْمَلُ أَحَدُهُمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِ، وَلَا عَلَى الْمَجْمُوعِ، وَلَيْسَ مَعْنَى الْحَدِّ بِهَذَا الِاعْتِبَارِ مَعْنَى الْمَحْدُودِ الْمَعْقُولِ ، لَكِنْ إِذَا لُوحِظَ إِلَى إِبْهَامِ أَحَدِهِمَا وَقُيِّدَ بِالْآخَرِ مُتَضَمَّناً فِيهِ وَوُصِفَ تَوْصِيفاً لِأَجْلِ التَّحْصِيلِ وَالتَّقْوِيمِ كَانَ شَيْئاً مُؤَدِّياً إِلَى الصُّورَةِ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةِ الَّتِي لِلْمَحْدُودِ، كَاسِباً لَهَا . مَثَلاً الْحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ فِي تَحْدِيدِ الْإِنْسَانِ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ ، هُوَ بِعَيْنِهِ الْحَيَوَانُ الَّذِي ذَلِكَ الْحَيَوَانُ بِعَيْنِهِ النَّاطِقُ، كَمَا أَنَّ الْعَقْدَ الْحَمْلِيَّ يُفِيدُ الصُّورَةَ الِاتِّحَادِيَّةَ الَّتِي لِلْمَوْضُوعِ مَعَ الْمَحْمُولِ فِي الْخَارِجِ، إِلَّا أَنَّ هُنَاكَ تَرْكِيباً خَبَرِيَّا ، فَفِيهِ حُكْمٌ ، وَهُنَا تَرْكِيبٌ تَقْيِيدِيٌّ ، يُفِيدُ تَصْوِيرَ الِاتِّحَادِ فَقَطْ ، فَمَجْمُوعُ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ الْمُتَعَلِّقَةِ بِالْأَجْزَاءِ تَفْصِيلاً هُوَ الْحَدُّ الْمُوَصِّلُ إِلَى التَّصَوُّرِ الْوَاحِدِ، الْمُتَعَلِّقِ بِجَمِيعِ الْأَجْزَاءِ إِجْمَالاً ، وَهُوَ الْمَحْدُودُ. فَانْدَفَعَ شَكُّ الرَّازِيِّ، مِنْ أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ إِمَّا بِنَفْسِهَا أَوْ بِجَمِيعِ أَجْزَائِهَا ، وَهُوَ نَفْسُهَا ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَحْصِيلُ الْحَاصِلِ، أَوْ بِالْعَوَارِضِ، وَلَا عِلْمَ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ إِلَّا الْعِلْمُ بِالْكُنْهِ، وَالْعَوَارِضُ لَا تُعْطِيهِ، فَالْأَقْسَامُ بِأَسْرِهَا بَاطِلَةٌ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا ذَهَبَ إِلَى بَدَاهَةِ التَّصَوُّرَاتِ كُلِّهَا.
Then here are investigations. The first: the genus, even if it is ambiguous, yet the mind may create for it, from the aspect of intellection, an isolated existence, and adds an increase to it, not that it is an external meaning adhering to it, but rather it restricts it for the sake of its attainment and specification, containing it. So when it becomes attained, it is not something else, for attainment does not change it but rather actualizes it. So if you look at the definition, you find it composed of several meanings, each of which is different from the other in some respect, so there is multiplicity in actuality, so one is not predicated of the other, nor of the whole, and the meaning of the definition in this respect is not the meaning of the defined intellect, but when the ambiguity of one is observed and it is restricted by the other, being contained in it and described with a description for the sake of attainment and correction, it becomes something leading to the unitary form of the defined, acquiring it. For example, 'rational animal' in defining man: one thing is understood from it, which is precisely the animal that is precisely the rational, just as the predicative proposition conveys the unitary form of the subject with the predicate in the external, except that there is a predicative composition, so there is judgment, while here it is a restrictive composition, conveying only the depiction of unity. So the totality of conceptualizations related to the parts in detail is the definition that leads to the single conceptualization related to all the parts as a whole, and that is the defined. Thus al-Razi's doubt is repelled: that the definition of the essence is either by itself or by all its parts, and it is itself, so definition is obtaining the already obtained, or by accidents, and there is no knowledge of reality except knowledge of the essence, and accidents do not give it, so all the divisions are false. And from here he went to the position that all conceptualizations are self-evident.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is the first of three investigations (mabahith) concerning definition. The author explains how the mind constructs a unified concept from the parts of a definition. The genus is initially ambiguous (mubham), but the mind creates an isolated existence for it and adds a differentia, not as an external addition but as a restriction to attain specificity. When the genus is restricted, it becomes actualized and not a different entity. In a definition like 'rational animal', the parts are multiple, but when one is restricted by the other, they form a unitary concept, analogous to a predicative proposition (like 'Man is rational') but without the judgment—here it is a restrictive composition (tarkib taqyidi) that merely depicts unity. The author then uses this to refute a doubt (shakk) attributed to al-Razi: that definition is either by the essence itself (which would be redundant) or by all its parts (which is the same) or by accidents (which cannot give essence). Al-Razi concluded that all conceptualizations are self-evident (badiha). The author argues that the definition leads to a unified concept from the parts, so it is not obtaining the already obtained.

Key Terms
التركيب التقييدي
التصوّر
الحد
المحصول
الشك الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between predicative composition and restrictive composition?
A) Predicative composition involves judgment; restrictive composition only depicts unity.
B) Predicative composition is for definitions; restrictive composition is for descriptions.
C) Restrictive composition is always false; predicative composition is always true.
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Definition is not redundant; it uses restrictive composition to unify multiple parts into a single, new conceptualization, thereby replying to al-Razi's objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a definition of 'zakāh' (obligatory charity) using genus and differentia, then explain how the differentia restricts the genus to form a unified concept, not a mere list.
?
Consider al-Razi's doubt applied to the Islamic legal definition of 'ribā'. Show how the restrictive composition model answers the objection that definition is redundant or impossible.

The Definitor as a Painter: Definition as Pure Conceptualization
Author likens definitor to painter drawing a form on a board.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the painter analogy and distinguish definition (taswīr) from judgment (ḥukm).

Core Idea
Definition is mental painting, not a verdict.

Classical logicians used vivid analogies to clarify the nature of definition. Here, the definitor is compared to a painter drawing a figure on a board. The point is that definition is pure conceptualization (tasawwur), devoid of any assertion or judgment. This distinction is critical because it separates the act of forming a concept from the act of making a claim about reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Find the two elements compared: the definitor (muʿarrij) and the painter (naqqāsh). What does the painter do? He paints a figure (shabaḥ) on a board (lawḥ).
The painter does not judge the figure; he merely depicts it.
2
Extract the key claim
The author states: 'So definition is pure depiction (taswīr), with no judgment in it.' This is the central insight. What does 'no judgment' mean? It means no assertion of truth or falsehood, no predication.
This aligns with the standard view that taṣawwur (conceptualization) precedes taṣdīq (assent).
3
Apply the analogy to a definition
Take a simple definition like 'Human is a rational animal'. According to the analogy, this definition does not assert that humans exist; it merely depicts the concept 'human' in the mind. The definition itself is neither true nor false—it is a mental form.
This protects definitions from certain objections that assume they are propositions.
4
Consider the incomplete phrase
The text ends with 'Yes, there is...' (naʿam, hunāka). What might the author be about to say? Possibly an exception or clarification that definitions can involve implicit judgments in some contexts.
This is a textual clue that the analogy may have limits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ: مَثَلُ الْمُعَرِّفِ كَمَثَلِ نَقَّاشٍ يَنْقُشُ شَبَحًا فِي التُّوحِ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَصْوِيرٌ بَحْتُ، لَا حُكْمَ فِيهِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمُنُوعِ نَعَمْ، هُنَاكَ
The third: the example of the definitor is like that of a painter who paints a figure on a board. So definition is pure depiction, with no judgment in it, so no objection from the prohibitions can be directed against it. Yes, there is...
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is the third investigation, presenting an analogy: the definitor (mu'arrij) is like a painter (naqqash) who paints a shape (shabah) on a board (tawh). The point is that definition is pure depiction (taswir) without any judgment (hukm). Therefore, no objection from the prohibitions (al-munu' — likely referring to the earlier conditions or objections) can be directed against it. The text cuts off, but it likely continues to discuss some exception or further point.

Key Terms
التصوير (al-taswīr): depiction; here used synonymously with conceptualization (taṣawwur).
المنوع (al-munūʿ): prohibitions or obstacles — likely referring to objections that apply to judgments but not to concepts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of the painter illustrate about definition?
A) Definition involves judgment
B) Definition is like painting a picture, pure depiction without judgment
C) Definition is only for composite things
D) Definition is always verbal

Key Takeaway
Definition is a mental picture, not a proposition — it contains no truth claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition 'Ghusl is washing the entire body with a specific intention' (from fiqh). Explain how this definition is pure depiction and does not include the judgment that ghusl is obligatory for janābah.
?
Consider the analogy: If someone objects 'But ghusl is not always required!' would that objection apply to the definition itself? Why or why not?
