Definition as Unitive Concept and Refutation of al-Razi's Doubt
Author defines definition as unitive conception and refutes al-Razi's skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a definition (hadd) conveys the essence as a unified whole and refute al-Razi's argument that all conceptions are axiomatic.

Core Idea
A definition does not add new content; it presents the same essence in a unitive form from its parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies the function of definition (hadd): it does not produce new knowledge of the essence but presents it as a unified whole from its detailed parts. He then refutes a doubt raised by al-Razi, who argued that definition is either redundant (acquiring what is already acquired) or impossible (since accidents cannot give essence). Understanding this refutation is crucial for appreciating the role of definition in Islamic logic and its use in sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of a definition
The author uses 'rational animal' for human. Note that the definition has parts (genus and differentia) but they are unified into one conception.
The parts are distinct in analysis but combined in the unified concept.
2
Compare definition with a predicative proposition
Both convey unity, but definition lacks judgment; it is purely conceptual, while a proposition includes a truth claim.
This distinction is key to understanding definition's role.
3
Follow al-Razi's doubt
Understand his three possibilities: definition by itself (redundant), by parts (same as itself), or by accidents (cannot give essence). He concludes all are invalid.
This leads him to claim all conceptions are axiomatic.
4
See how the author refutes it
The definition does not acquire new content but organizes existing conceptions into a unitive whole. Thus it is not redundant.
The refutation preserves the usefulness of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوُصِفَ تَوْصِيفًا لِأَجْلِ التَّحْصِيلِ وَالتَّقْويمِ كَانَ شَيْئًا مُؤَدِّيَا إِلَى الصُّورَةِ الْوَحْدَانِيَّةِ الَّتِي لِلْمَحْدُودِ، كَاسِبًا لَهَا . مَثَلًا الْحَيَوَانُ النَّاطِقُ فِي تَحْدِيدِ الْإِنْسَانِ يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ ، هُوَ بِعَيْنِهِ الْحَيَوَانُ الَّذِي ذَلِكَ الْحَيَوَانُ بِعَيْنِهِ النَّاطِقُ، كَمَا أَنَّ الْعَقْدَ الْحَمْلِي يُفيدُ الصُّورَةَ الاِتِّحَادِيَّةَ الَّتِي لِلْمَوْضُوع مَعَ الْمَحْمُولِ فِي الْخَارِج، إِلَّا أَنَّ هُنَاكَ تَرْكِيبًا خَبَرِيَّا ، فَفِيهِ حُكْمٌ ، وَهُنَا تَرْكِيبٌ تَفْيِيدِي ، يُفيدُ تَصْوِير الاتِّحَادِ فَقَطْ ، فَمَجْمُوعُ التَّصَرُّرَاتِ الْمُتَعَلَّقَةِ بِالْأَجْزَاءِ تَفْصِيلًا هُوَ الْحَدُّ الْمُوَصِّلُ إِلَى التَّصَوُّرِ الْوَاحِدِ، الْمُتَعَلِّقِ بِجَمِيعِ الْأَجْزَاءِ إِجْمَالا ، وَهُوَ الْمَحْدُودُ. فَانْدَفَعَ شَكٍّ الرَّازِيِّ، مِنْ أَنَّ تَعْرِيفَ الْمَاهِيَّةِ إِمَّا بِنَفْسِهَا أَوْ بِجَمِيع أَجْزَائِهَا ، وَهُوَ نَفْسُهَا ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَحْصِيلُ الْحَاصِلِ، أَوْ بِالْعَوَارِضِ، وَلَا عِلْمَ بِالْحَقِيقَةِ إِلَّا الْعِلْمُ بِالْكُنْهِ، وَالْعَوَارِضُ لَا تُعْطِيهِ، فَالْأَقْسَامُ بِأَسْرِهَا بَاطِلَةٌ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا ذَهَبَ إِلَى بَدَاهَةِ التَّصَورَاتِ كُلِّهَا.
And it was described by a description for the sake of acquisition and rectification, it is something leading to the unitive form that belongs to the defined, acquiring it. For example, 'rational animal' in the definition of 'human' is understood from it one thing, which is precisely the animal that is precisely the rational one, just as the predicative proposition conveys the unitive form of the subject with the predicate in reality, except that there is a predicative composition, so in it there is a judgment, while here there is a descriptive composition, conveying only the depiction of unity. So the sum of the conceptions related to the parts in detail is the definition (hadd) that leads to the single conception related to all the parts in summary, which is the defined. Thus the doubt of al-Razi is repelled, that the definition of the essence is either by itself or by all its parts, which is itself, so definition would be acquisition of the already acquired; or by accidents, but there is no knowledge of the reality except knowledge of the inner nature, and accidents do not give it, so all the divisions are invalid. And from here he went to the position that all conceptions are axiomatic (badihiyya).
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of definition (hadd). It begins by comparing definition to a description that leads to a unitive form of the defined, i.e., the definition expresses the essence as a single concept. The example of 'rational animal' for human illustrates that the definition yields one thing: the animal that is precisely rational. This is analogous to a predicative proposition that shows unity between subject and predicate, but with a difference: the proposition includes a judgment (tasdiq), while the definition (hadd) is a descriptive composition that only depicts unity, without judgment. The sum of the detailed conceptions of the parts is the definition that leads to the single total conception of the defined. Then the author mentions a doubt by al-Razi: definition of the essence is either (a) by itself or by all its parts, which is the same as the essence, so it is acquiring what is already present (تحصيل الحاصل); or (b) by accidents, which cannot give knowledge of the inner reality. Thus all divisions are invalid, leading al-Razi to claim that all conceptions are axiomatic. The author refutes this: the definition does not produce new knowledge of the essence but presents it in a different form (from detailed to total conception). So al-Razi's doubt is repelled.

Key Terms
الحد
التصاوير
تحصيل الحاصل
بداهة التصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between a definition (hadd) and a predicative proposition (qadiya hamliyya)?
A) Definition is composed of parts; proposition is not.
B) Definition conveys unity without judgment; proposition includes judgment.
C) Definition is about essence; proposition is about existence.
D) Definition is always true; proposition can be false.

Key Takeaway
Definition repackages the detailed parts of an essence into a single conception, thus al-Razi's objection that it is redundant is mistaken.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Verbal Definition as Conceptual Demand
Author distinguishes verbal definition from linguistic inquiry about word usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a verbal definition (conceptual demand) and a linguistic inquiry about word usage (assensual demand).

Core Idea
Explaining a word's meaning is conceptual; proving its lexical usage is assensual.

This passage clarifies that verbal definition is a conceptual demand, not an assensual one. The author uses the example of 'ghadafar' to show that explaining what a word means does not involve making a judgment, whereas proving that the word is actually used for that meaning is a separate, linguistic inquiry that requires evidence. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion in logical and linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of demands
Recall that logical demands are either conceptual (tasawwuri) – seeking a conception without judgment, or assensual (tasdiqi) – seeking assent or judgment. Verbal definition is claimed to be conceptual.
Conceptual demands answer 'what is it?'; assensual demands answer 'whether it is?'
2
Analyze the example given
The author says: 'If we say: The ghadafar exists, and the interlocutor asks: What is the ghadafar? and we explain it as the lion, there is no judgment.' The explanation of the word 'ghadafar' as 'lion' is purely conceptual – it provides a meaning without affirming or denying anything about existence.
Note that the original statement 'the ghadafar exists' is a judgment, but the definition itself is not.
3
Contrast with linguistic inquiry
Proving that the word 'ghadafar' is actually used for the meaning 'lion' is a different matter: it is a linguistic inquiry (bahth lughawi) that requires evidence from the science of language. This is assensual because it seeks to establish a fact about language.
The author criticizes those who confuse these two: they treat a definition as if it requires proof, or treat a proof of usage as if it were a simple definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّانِي : التَّعْرِيفُ اللَّفْظِيُّ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصَوُّرِيَّةِ ؛ فَإِنَّهُ جَوَابُ «مَا» ، وَكُلُّ مَا هُوَ جَوَاب «ما» فَهُوَ تَصَوُّرٌ، أَلَا تَرَى إِذَا قُلْنَا: الْغَضَنْفَرُ مَوْجُودٌ، فَقَالَ الْمُخَاطَبُ مَا الْغَضَنَفَرُ ؟ فَفَسَّرْنَاهُ بِالْأَسَدِ، فَلَيْسَ هُنَاكَ حُكْمٌ. نَعَمْ، بَيَانُ مَوْضُوعِيَّةِ اللَّفْظِ فِي جَوَابِ هَلْ هَذَا اللَّفْظُ مَوْضُوعٌ لِمَعْنَى بَحْثُ لَفْظِيٌّ ، يُقْصَدُ إِثْبَاتُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فِي عِلْمِ اللُّغَةِ، فَمَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ مِنَ الْمَطَالِبِ التَّصْدِيقِيَّةِ لَمْ يُفَرِّقُ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الْبَحْثِ اللفظي اللُّغَوِي.
The second: verbal definition is from the conceptual demands; for it is the answer to 'what is it?', and everything that is an answer to 'what is it?' is a conception. Do you not see that if we say: 'The ghadafar exists', and the interlocutor says: 'What is the ghadafar?' and we explain it as 'the lion', there is no judgment. Yes, the demonstration of the lexical usage of a word in answering 'Is this word used for a meaning?' is a linguistic inquiry, intended to be proved by evidence in the science of language. So whoever says it is from the assensual demands did not distinguish between it and the linguistic inquiry.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi) as a conceptual demand (matlab tasawwuri), not an assensual one (tasdiqi). The author argues that answering the question 'what is it?' (مَا) gives a conception, not a judgment. For example, if we say 'The ghadafar exists' and someone asks 'What is ghadafar?' we answer 'the lion'. This explanation does not include a judgment. However, proving that the word 'ghadafar' is actually used for a meaning (i.e., that it has a lexical usage) is a different matter: it is a linguistic inquiry (bahth lughawi) that requires evidence and is therefore a tasdiqi matter. The author criticizes those who confuse the two: defining a word by its meaning is tasawwuri, while proving its lexicographical reality is tasdiqi.

Key Terms
التعريف اللفظي
المطلب التصوُّري
المطلب التصديقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is verbal definition a conceptual demand?
A) Because it requires evidence from the science of language.
B) Because it answers 'what is it?' without making a judgment.
C) Because it is a judgment that the word means something.
D) Because it is a linguistic inquiry.

Key Takeaway
Verbal definition provides a conception, not a judgment; proving the lexical reality of a word is a separate assensual inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as a conceptual demand (tasawwuri) or an assensual demand (tasdiqi): (a) 'Zakat is a specific amount of wealth given to specific categories.' (b) 'Zakat is obligatory based on Quran 9:60.'
?
Explain why proving that a word is used for a meaning (e.g., that 'ghadafar' means lion) is assensual, but explaining the meaning itself is conceptual.

The Definer as Sculptor: Definition as Pure Conceptualization
Author compares definer to sculptor and explains definition as pure conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the sculptor analogy to explain why a definition is not a judgment and cannot be directly objected to.

Core Idea
Definition is pure conceptualization, not a judgment.

In this passage, the author compares the definer to a sculptor carving a figure, illustrating that definition is simply a conceptualization (taswir) and contains no inherent judgment. This understanding is crucial for recognizing why objections to definitions must target implicit claims rather than the definition itself.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the analogy
Read the sentence: 'The likeness of the definer is like a sculptor carving a figure on a board.' Understand that the definer is actively shaping the concept, just as the sculptor shapes the figure.
The board (tawh) represents the mind, and the carving is the definition.
2
Identify the nature of definition
The author says definition is 'pure conceptualization' (taswir bahth) with 'no judgment in it.' This means it does not assert truth or falsehood; it simply presents the essence.
Contrast with a proposition (qadiyya) which contains judgment.
3
Understand why no objection is directed at it
Since there is no judgment, there is nothing to dispute about the definition itself. Objections (al-munu') are not applicable to the conceptualization, only to the claims that accompany it.
This is a key principle in jadal: definitions are accepted as given, but their implications can be challenged.
4
Connect to the implicit claims
The author introduces 'ahkam dimniyya' (implicit rulings) that accompany the definition. These are contestable. This step sets up the next lesson on those claims.
Examples: the claim that the definition captures the essence (haddiyya) or is comprehensive (itrad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ: مَثَلُ الْمُعَرِّفِ كَمَثَلِ نَقَّاشٍ يَنْقُسُ شَبَحًا فِي التَّوْحِ، فَالتَّعْرِيفُ تَصْوِيرٌ بَحْتُ، لَا حُكْمَ فِيهِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْمُنُوعِ نَعَمْ، هُنَاكَ أَحْكَامُ ضِمْنِيَّةٌ ، مِثْلَ دَعْوَى الْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْمَفْهُومِيَّةِ وَالإطْرَادِ وَالإِنْعِكَاسِ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ ، فَيَجُوزُ مَنْعُ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ ، لَكِنَّ الْعُلَمَاءَ أَجْمَعُوا عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْعَ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لَا يَجُوزُ، فَكَأَنَّهُ شَرِيعَةٌ نُسِخَتْ قَبْلَ الْعَمَلِ بِهَا ! نَعَمْ، يُنْقَصُ بِإِبْطَالِ الطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْسِ مَثَلًا، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ إِنَّمَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْحُدُودِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ ؛ إِذْ حَقِيقَةُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا وَاحِدًا، بِخِلَافِ الرُّسُومِ.
The third: the likeness of the definer is like a sculptor carving a figure on a board; so definition is pure conceptualization, no judgment in it, so nothing of the objections is directed at it. Yes, there are implicit rulings, such as the claim of being definitional (haddiyya), conceptual (mafhumiyya), comprehensiveness (itrad), and exclusiveness (in'ikas), and so on. So it is permissible to deny those rulings, but the scholars have agreed that denying definitions themselves is not permissible, as if it were a law that was abrogated before acting upon it! Yes, it is criticized by invalidating the universal comprehensiveness and the converse, for example. And opposition is only conceivable in true definitions, since the reality of a thing is only one, unlike descriptions (rusum).
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of definition further. The definer is likened to a sculptor carving a shape on a board: definition is pure conceptualization (taswir) without any judgment. Therefore, one cannot object to a definition itself. However, implicit claims (ahkam dimniyya) accompany definitions: e.g., that the definition is truly the essence (haddiyya), that it conveys the meaning (mafhumiyya), that it is coextensive (itrad) and exclusive (in'ikas). These implicit claims can be contested. The scholars have agreed that rejecting a definition itself is impermissible, but it is permissible to challenge these claims. Then the author notes that opposition (mu'arada) is only conceived in true definitions (hudud haqiqiyya), because a thing's reality is only one, unlike descriptions (rusum) which can have multiple correct ones.

Key Terms
الدعوى الحدية (al-da'wa al-haddiyya): The claim that a given definition captures the essence of the thing.
الإطراد (al-itrad): Comprehensiveness: the definition applies to all instances of the defined.
الإنعكاس (al-in'ikas): Exclusiveness: the definition applies only to instances of the defined.
الرسم (al-rasm): Description: a non-essential definition that may not be unique.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Pure conceptualization without judgment
C) A combination of conceptualization and judgment
D) A claim of comprehensiveness

Key Takeaway
Definition is pure conceptualization without judgment; objections target implicit claims, not the definition itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Explain why objecting to the definition itself is not permissible according to this passage. Then, identify one implicit claim that could be contested (e.g., that it is comprehensive of all mujtahids) and explain how you would challenge it.

The Implicit Rulings of Definitions: What Can Be Contested
Author explains implicit rulings accompanying definitions and the scholars' agreement on contestability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four implicit claims (da'wa haddiyya, mafhumiyya, itrad, in'ikas) and explain why scholars allow denying these but not the definition itself.

Core Idea
Implicit claims in definitions can be challenged; the definition itself is sacrosanct.

After establishing that definition is pure conceptualization without judgment, the author introduces the implicit rulings (ahkam dimniyya) that accompany every definition. These include claims that it captures the essence (haddiyya), conveys the meaning (mafhumiyya), is comprehensive (itrad), and exclusive (in'ikas). Scholars agreed that these claims can be denied, but the definition itself cannot—as if it were an abrogated law. This lesson focuses on understanding these implicit claims and their role in critical debate.

Guided Path
1
List the implicit claims
From the text, extract the four explicit claims: da'wa haddiyya (definitional), mafhumiyya (conceptual), itrad (comprehensiveness), in'ikas (exclusiveness). There may be others (ila ghayri dhalik).
These are the 'ahkam dimniyya' (implicit rulings).
2
Understand each claim
Da'wa haddiyya: the definition truly gives the essence. Mafhumiyya: the definition conveys the intended meaning. Itrad: the definition covers all instances. In'ikas: the definition excludes non-instances.
These are logical conditions for a good definition.
3
Recognize the scholars' agreement
The author states that scholars agree (ajma'u) that 'denying definitions themselves' is not permissible, but it is permissible to deny these implicit rulings. This is a crucial principle in the science of definition.
Compare to an abrogated law: the definition is like a revealed text that cannot be rejected, but its application can be debated.
4
Distinguish between true definitions and descriptions
Opposition (mu'arada) is only conceivable in true definitions (hudud haqiqiyya) because each thing has only one reality. Descriptions (rusum) can have multiple correct ones, so opposition may not apply.
This limits where debates about definitions can occur.
5
Apply to a real example
Consider the definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) in Usul al-Fiqh. Identify the implicit claims and think of an objection to, say, its exclusiveness (in'ikas) by providing a counterexample.
Practice transferring this theoretical understanding to actual Islamic sciences.

Why Single Words Cannot Indicate Detail
Author argues that a single word cannot provide detailed meaning, so definition by a single word is only verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal definition and real definition based on the nature of single words.

Core Idea
A single word can only bring a concept to mind, not detail its parts.

In Islamic logic, definitions are crucial for precise understanding. A common mistake is to assume that a single word, like 'human', gives detailed knowledge of the essence. This lesson explores why a single word cannot indicate detail, and what that means for definitions.

Guided Path
1
Define 'single word' and 'composite speech'
A single word (al-lafz al-mufrad) is a word that is not composed of meaningful parts, e.g., 'book'. Composite speech combines multiple words, e.g., 'a useful book'. The passage begins by stating that a single word does not indicate detail at all.
2
Understand the consequence of detail in a single word
If a single word did indicate detail, then a proposition consisting of a single word (qadiyya ahadhiyya) would be possible. But logically, a proposition requires a subject and predicate; a single word cannot be a full proposition. Thus, the initial assumption is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Recognize the implication for definitions
When a single word is defined by a composite expression (e.g., 'human' defined as 'rational animal'), the detail from the composite is not part of the meaning of the single word; it merely clarifies. This is a verbal definition (al-ta'rif al-lafzi), not a real one.
Verbal definitions explain the term, not the essence.
4
Apply the analogy to simple concepts
The passage notes that words are like simple concepts: they have no composition, truth, or falsity. They only bring the concept to mind. If a single word already contained detail, defining it by a composite would cause a vicious circle.
This reinforces that real definition requires composite expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : اللَّفْظُ الْمُفْرَدُ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ أَصْلًا، وَإِلَّا لَجَازَ تَحْقُقُ فَضِيَّةٍ آحَادِيَّةٍ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا قَالُوا: الْمُفْرَدُ إِذَا عُرِّفَ بِمُرَكَّبٍ تَعْرِيفًا لَفْظِيًّا لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّفْصِيلُ الْمُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ الْمُرَكَّبِ مَقْصُودًا . قَالَ الشَّيْخُ الْأَسْمَاءُ وَالْكَلِمُ فِي الْأَلْفَاظِ نَظِيرُ الْمَعْقُولَاتِ الْمُفْرَدَةِ الَّتِي لَا تَفْصِيلَ فِيهَا، وَلَا تَرْكِيبَ وَلَا صِدْقَ وَلَا كَذِبَ، بَلْ لَا يُفيدُ الْمَعْنَى ، وَإِنَّمَا مِنْهُ الْإِحْضَارُ فَقَطْ ، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الدَّوْرُ ، فَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِهِ إِلَّا لَفْظِيَّا.
The fourth: the single word does not indicate detail at all; otherwise, a single-word proposition would be possible. And from here they said: when a single word is defined by a composite in a verbal definition, the detail derived from that composite is not intended. Al-Shaykh said: the nouns and verbs among words are analogous to the single concepts that have no detail, no composition, and no truth or falsity; rather, they do not convey meaning, but only serve to bring it to mind. Otherwise, a vicious circle would arise; so definition by it is only valid as a verbal definition.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth point about definitions: a single word (lafz mufrad) cannot indicate detailed meaning. If it did, then a one-word proposition (qadiyya ahadhiyya) would be valid, but that is impossible because a proposition requires a subject and predicate. Therefore, when a single word is defined by a composite expression in a verbal definition, the detailed meaning from the composite is not the intended meaning of the single word—it is merely to clarify the referent. Al-Shaykh (likely al-Sanusi or an earlier authority) confirms that words are like simple concepts: they have no detail, composition, truth or falsity; they only bring the meaning to mind. If they did indicate detail, a vicious circle would occur (since defining a single word by a composite would reduce to the same level). Hence, definition by a single word is only valid as a verbal definition, not a real one.

Key Terms
اللفظ المفرد
قضية آحادية
التعريف اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be possible if a single word indicated detail?
A) A circular definition would occur.
B) A single-word proposition would be possible.
C) A verbal definition would become real.
D) The word would become composite.

Key Takeaway
A single word cannot convey detailed meaning; therefore, any definition using a single word is only verbal, not real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Denying Implicit Judgments vs. Denying Definitions
Author states the consensus that objecting to definitions is prohibited like abrogated law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between implicit judgments accompanying a definition and the definition itself, and explain why only the former can be objected to.

Core Idea
Objections to definitions are prohibited; only their implicit claims may be challenged.

When someone presents a definition, it carries with it several implicit claims—for example, that it captures the essence (haddiyya), that it conveys the concept (mafhumiyya), that it covers all instances (itrad), and that it is convertible (in'ikas). This lesson examines the rule: one may object to those implicit claims, but the definition itself is immune from outright denial. Scholars have agreed on this, likening the rejection of a definition to a law abrogated before it could be acted upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implicit judgments
Locate the four implicit judgments mentioned: al-haddiyya (essentiality), al-mafhumiyya (conceptuality), al-itrad (coextensiveness), and al-in'ikas (convertibility). Understand each as a claim about the definition.
These are the 'ahkam dimniyya' that can be challenged.
2
Understand the prohibition on denying definitions
The author states that scholars unanimously agree (ajma'u) that denying the definition itself (man' al-ta'rifat) is not allowed. The analogy: it is like a law abrogated before implementation—pointless.
The definition is a given; objections target its accompanying claims, not its existence.
3
Apply the distinction to a real definition
Take a definition from Islamic sciences, e.g., 'Wine (khamr) is any intoxicating drink' (a hadd). Identify one implicit judgment (e.g., coextensiveness: all intoxicating drinks are called wine). Ask: can you deny that the definition is coextensive? Yes. Can you deny that wine is defined as intoxicating drink? No, according to this rule.
Practise this distinction to internalize the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَحْكَامُ ضِمْنِيَّةٌ ، مِثْلَ دَعْوَى الْحَدِّيَّةِ وَالْمَفْهُومِيَّةِ وَالإطْرَادِ وَالإِنْعِكَاسِ إِلَى غَيْرِ ذَلِكَ ، فَيَجُوزُ مَنْعُ تِلْكَ الْأَحْكَامِ ، لَكِنَّ الْعُلَمَاءَ أَجْمَعُوا عَلَى أَنَّ مَنْعَ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ لَا يَجُوزُ، فَكَأَنَّهُ شَرِيعَةٌ نُسِخَتْ قَبْلَ الْعَمَلِ بِهَا !
Implicit judgments, such as the claim of essentiality and conceptuality and coextensiveness and converse, etc., so it is permissible to deny those judgments, but the scholars have agreed that denying definitions is not allowed, as if it is a law abrogated before acting upon it!
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage clarifies the rules of objection (man') in definitions. The author distinguishes between implicit judgments (ahkam dimniyya) that accompany definitions—like the claim that the definition is essential (had'diyya), that it captures the concept (mafhumiyya), that it is coextensive (itrad), and that it is convertible (in'ikas)—and the definition itself. One may object to those implicit judgments, but the scholars have unanimously agreed that rejecting the definition outright is impermissible. The author likens such rejection to a law that was abrogated before it could be acted upon, emphasizing its futility. This highlights the authority of definitions in logic; they are not subject to arbitrary denial.

الإطراد
الإنعكاس
التعاريف

D) Because definitions are part of divine revelation.

Key Takeaway
The definition itself is immune from outright denial; only its accompanying implicit claims may be contested, as per scholarly consensus.

Opposition Only in Real Definitions
Author explains that mu'arada (opposition) applies only to real definitions, not descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real definitions and descriptions regarding the applicability of opposition (mu'arada).

Core Idea
Opposition only applies to real definitions because each essence is unique.

When we define a thing, not every definition can be challenged by presenting an alternative. The author explains that this challenge, called mu'arada, only makes sense when the definition aims at the essence of the thing. Real definitions (al-hudud al-haqiqiyya) capture that single essence, so only one correct definition exists. In contrast, descriptions (al-rusum) can be multiple, so opposition is irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Understand mu'arada
Mu'arada means presenting an alternative definition to challenge the original. The author says a definition can be flawed if it fails coextensiveness (tard) or converse ('aks).
This is a common method to test definitions.
2
Distinguish real definitions from descriptions
Real definitions capture the essence (haqiqa) of a thing; descriptions capture accidental properties. The author contrasts them: essence is one, descriptions can vary.
Think of the difference between 'human as rational animal' (real) vs 'human as bipedal' (description).
3
Grasp the uniqueness of essence
The author states 'the essence of a thing is only one' (haqiqat al-shay' la yakun illa wahidan). This is why multiple real definitions cannot coexist – you cannot have two different essences for the same thing.
If someone proposes a different real definition, at most one can be correct; thus opposition is meaningful.
4
Apply the rule
Given a definition, decide if it is real or descriptive. If real, mu'arada is conceivable; if descriptive, opposition is not a valid test.
Use the test: does it capture the essence or only accidents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نَعَمْ، يُنْقَصُ بِإِبْطَالِ الطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْسِ مَثَلًا، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ إِنَّمَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْحُدُودِ الْحَقِيقِيَّةِ ; إِذْ حَقِيقَةُ الشَّيْءِ لَا يَكُونُ إِلَّا وَاحِدًا، بِخِلَافِ الرُّسُومِ.
Yes, it is flawed by the invalidation of coextensiveness and converse, for example, and opposition is only conceivable in real definitions, because the essence of a thing is only one, unlike descriptions.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of opposition (mu'arada) in definitions. The author notes that a definition can be flawed (yunqasu) by invalidating its coextensiveness (tard) and converse (aks). However, opposition (mu'arada) — presenting an alternative definition — is only conceivable for real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya), because a thing's essence (haqiqa) is unique and cannot have multiple real definitions. In contrast, descriptions (rusum) are not unique; a thing can have multiple correct descriptions, so opposition is not relevant there. This reinforces the idea that real definitions aim at capturing the single essence.

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Opposition: presenting a rival definition to challenge the original.
الحدود الحقيقية (al-hudud al-haqiqiyya): Real definitions that capture the essence (dhat) of a thing.
الرسوم (al-rusum): Descriptions that capture accidental properties rather than essence.
الطرد (al-tard): Coextensiveness: the definition applies to all instances of the definiendum.
العكس (al-'aks): Converse: the definiendum applies to all instances of the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because real definitions are longer.
D) Because opposition is not a valid logical tool.

Key Takeaway
Opposition (mu'arada) is only meaningful for real definitions because each thing's essence is singular.

Verbal Definition of Simple Terms
Author explains that simple terms lack detail, so verbal definition only brings the term to mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) from real definition (hadd haqiqi) for simple terms and explain why circularity would result otherwise.

Core Idea
Simple terms only bring the concept to mind; their definitions are merely verbal, not analytical.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle issue: how can we define simple terms (mufrad) that by nature indicate no internal composition? He argues that any compound definition of a simple term must be understood as a verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) that merely reminds the hearer of the term's meaning, not as a real definition that reveals its essence. If we insisted that the compound provided new detailed knowledge, we would fall into circularity (dawr). Let's unpack his reasoning and see how this principle applies in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage focuses on the term 'al-mufrad' (simple term). Note that it is a single word indicating one concept without composition.
2
Understand the property of simple terms
Simple terms indicate no detail (tafsil) at all. They are like simple concepts (ma'qulat mufrada) with no composition, truth, or falsehood; their function is merely to bring the concept to mind (ihdar).
This is contrasted with compound terms or propositions which do carry detail.
3
Examine the definition type
When a simple term is defined by a compound expression, the detail from the compound is not intended. The definition is verbal (lafzi) – it only clarifies conventional usage, not essence.
The author emphasizes that the detail in the compound is not what the definition seeks to convey.
4
Recognize the consequence of ignoring this
If the compound definition were taken to provide new detailed knowledge of the simple term, circularity (dawr) would follow because the simple term's meaning would be presupposed to understand the compound.
This is the key logical argument: avoiding circularity requires limiting definitions of simple terms to verbal ones.
5
Apply the rule
Therefore, only verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi) is valid for simple terms. Real definitions (hadd) are impossible because they would require composition and detail that simple terms do not possess.
This distinction is crucial when reading definitions in Islamic scholarly works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : اللَّفْظُ الْمُفْرَدُ لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى التَّفْصِيلِ أَصْلًا، وَإِلَّا لَجَازَ تَحْقُقُ فَضِيَّةٍ آحَادِيَّةٍ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا قَالُوا: الْمُفْرَدُ إِذَا عُرِّفَ بِمُرَكَّبٍ تَعْرِيفًا لَفْظِيًّا لَمْ يَكُنِ التَّفْصِيلُ الْمُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ الْمُرَكَّبِ مَقْصُودًا . قَالَ الشَّيْخُ الْأَسْمَاءُ وَالْكَلِمُ فِي الْأَلْفَاظِ نَظِيرُ الْمَعْقُولَاتِ الْمُفْرَدَةِ الَّتِي لَا تَفْصِيلَ فِيهَا، وَلَا تَرْكِيبَ وَلَا صِدْقَ وَلَا كَذِبَ، بَلْ لَا يُفيدُ الْمَعْنَى ، وَإِنَّمَا مِنْهُ الْإِحْضَارُ فَقَطْ ، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ الدَّوْرُ ، فَلَا يَصِحُ التَّعْرِيفُ بِهِ إِلَّا لَفْظِيَّا.
The fourth: The simple term does not indicate detail at all, otherwise a singular proposition would be actualized. Hence they said: When a simple term is defined by a compound in a verbal definition, the detail derived from that compound is not intended. The Shaykh said: Names and words in utterances are analogous to simple concepts that have no detail, no composition, no truth and no falsehood; rather they do not convey meaning, but only bring to mind, otherwise circularity would follow, so definition by it is only valid as verbal.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is the fourth point in a series about definitions. It concerns the definition of simple terms (mufrad). The author argues that a simple term does not indicate detail (tafsil) by itself; if it did, that would imply that a single term could constitute a complete proposition (qadiyya ahad? possibly 'singular proposition'). Because simple terms are like simple concepts (ma'qulat mufrada) that have no composition and are neither true nor false. When we define a simple term by a compound expression in a verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi), the detail provided by the compound is not the intended meaning; the definition merely brings the term to mind (ihdar). If it were otherwise, circularity (dawr) would result. Therefore, only verbal definition is valid for simple terms.

Key Terms
المفرد
التعريف اللفظي
الدور
الإحضار

B) To describe its essential qualities.
C) To bring the term to mind (ihdar) without detail.
D) To prove the existence of the term's referent.

Key Takeaway
Simple terms can only be defined verbally (lafzi) to avoid circularity; their definitions merely bring the concept to mind, not provide detailed knowledge.

The Modes of Predication in Islamic Logic
Author defines predication and classifies its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the different types of predication (primary/common, essential/accidental, derivative/equivalence) in propositions from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Predication is not one thing—its modes determine whether a proposition yields new knowledge or is self-evident.

This passage defines predication as a union of two distinct concepts and then systematically classifies its modes. The most important distinction is between primary predication (where subject and predicate are conceptually identical) and common predication (where they differ but share existence). Only common predication is informative and used in the sciences. Understanding these divisions helps avoid errors in reasoning about contradiction and definition.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition of haml
The definition 'اتحاد المتغايرين في نحو من التعقل بحسب نحو آخر من الوجود' means a union of two distinct things in some mode of intellection according to another mode of existence. Notice the two aspects: intellection (concept) and existence. This union can be essential or accidental.
The 'mode of existence' here refers to the external reality or mental existence.
2
Contrast primary and common predication
Primary predication (الحمل الأولى) occurs when the subject is precisely the predicate in concept, e.g., 'Human is human'. Common predication (الحمل الشائع) occurs when they differ conceptually but unite in existence, e.g., 'Human is an animal'. Only common predication conveys new information.
The passage says common predication is 'المعتبر في العلوم' (the one considered in the sciences).
3
Examine the divisions: essential vs accidental and derivative vs equivalence
Predication divides by whether the predicate is essential (ذاتي) or accidental (عرضي) to the subject. It also divides by the relation: derivative predication uses a preposition like 'في' or 'ذو' (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' meaning 'Zayd has standing'), while equivalence predication has no mediation (e.g., 'Zayd is a human').
The author suggests the term 'predication' is homonymous between derivative and equivalence types.
4
Apply the unity of mode condition to contradiction
The passage explains that some concepts (like 'concept' and 'possible') can be predicated of themselves even by common predication, while others (like 'partial') cannot—their opposites are predicated. This leads to an extra condition for contradiction: the mode of predication must be unified, in addition to the eight standard conditions.
The famous doubt (that predication seems impossible because subject and predicate concepts are identical) is resolved by distinguishing primary from common predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ الْحَمْلُ اتَّحَادُ الْمُتَغَابِرَيْنِ في نَحْوِ مِنَ التَّعَقُلِ بِحَسَبِ نَحْوِ آخَرَ مِنَ الْوُجُودِ، اتِّحَادًا بِالذَّاتِ، أَوْ بِالْعَرَضِ. وَهُوَ إِمَّا أَنْ يُعْنَى بِهِ أَنَّ الْمَوْضُوعَ بِعَيْنِهِ الْمَحْمُولُ، فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الْأَوْلَى ، وَقَدْ يَكُونَ نَظْرِيَّا أَيْضًا، أَوْ يُقْتَصَرُ فِيهِ عَلَى مُجَرَّدِ الإِنْحَادِ فِي الْوُجُودِ ، فَيُسَمَّى الْحَمْلَ الشَّائِعَ الْمُتَعَارَفَ، وَهُوَ الْمُعْتَبرُ فِي الْعُلُومِ. وَيَنْقَسِمُ بِحَسَبِ كَوْنِ الْمَحْمُولِ ذَاتِيَّا ، أَوْ عَرَضِيًّا لَهُ إِلَى الْحَمْلِ بِالذَّاتِ أَوْ بِالْعَرَضِ ، وَقَدْ يَنْقَسِمُ بِأَنَّ نِسْبَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ إِلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ إِمَّا بِوَاسِطَةِ «في» «ذُو» أَوْ «لَهُ» ، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالِاشْتِقَاقِ ، أَوْ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ، وَهُوَ الْمَقُولُ بِعَلَى، فَهُوَ الْحَمْلُ بِالْمُوَاطَاةِ ، وَالْأَشْبَهُ أَنَّ إِطْلَاقِ الْحَمْلِ عَلَيْهِمَا بِالاشْتِرَاكِ اللَّفْظِيُّ. اعْلَمْ أَنَّ كُلَّ مَنْهُومٍ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِالْحَمْلِ الْأَوْلَى، وَمِنْ هُنَاكَ تَسْمَعُ أَنَّ سَلْبَ الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ مُحَالٌ . ثُمَّ طَائِفَةٌ مِنَ الْمَفْهُومَاتِ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا حَمْلاً شَائِعًا أَيْضًا ، كَالْمَفْهُومِ وَالْمُمْكِنِ الْعَام وَنَحْوِهِمَا، وَطَائِفَةٌ لاَ يُحْمَلُ عَلَى أَنْفُسِهَا ذَلِكَ الْحَمْلَ، بَلْ يُحْمَلُ عَلَيْهَا نَقَائِضُهَا ، كَالْجُزْنِي وَاللأَمَفهُومِ. وَمِنْ هَهُنَا اعْتُبِرَ فِي التَّنَاقُضِ اتَّحَادُ نَحْوِ الْحَمْلِ فَوْقَ الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِ الذَّائِعَاتِ . وَهَهُنَا شَكٌ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ : أَنَّ الْحَمْلَ مُحَالٌ ؛ لِأَنَّ مَنْهُومَ (جَ) عَيْنُ مَنْهُومٍ
The third: Predication is the union of two distinct things in some mode of intellection according to another mode of existence, union by essence or by accident. It is either that the subject is precisely the predicate, called primary predication, and it may also be theoretical; or it is confined to mere union in existence, called common customary predication, which is the one considered in the sciences. And it divides according to whether the predicate is essential or accidental to it into predication by essence or by accident. And it may also divide by the relation of the predicate to the subject being either with a mediation like 'fi', 'dhu', or 'lahu', which is derivative predication, or without mediation, which is said by 'ala', which is predication by equivalence. And it is more likely that the term 'predication' is applied to both by homonymy. Know that every concept is predicated of itself by primary predication, and from that you hear that negating something of itself is impossible. Then a group of concepts are predicated of themselves by common predication as well, like 'concept' and 'general possible' and the like. And a group are not predicated of themselves by that predication, rather their opposites are predicated of them, like 'partial' and 'non-concept'. From this, the consideration in contradiction of the unity of the mode of predication beyond the eight well-known unities is taken. And here is a famous doubt, which is: that predication is impossible, because the concept of ج is identical to the concept of...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition and classification of predication (al-haml). Predication is defined as the union of two distinct entities in some mode of intellection (تعقل) with respect to another mode of existence—a union that can be essential or accidental. The author distinguishes two main types: primary predication (الحمل الأولى), where the subject is exactly the predicate (e.g., 'Human is human'), and common customary predication (الحمل الشائع المتعارف), where the subject and predicate share existence but are conceptually distinct (e.g., 'Human is an animal'). Only the latter is used in objective sciences because it conveys new information. Predication is further divided based on whether the predicate is essential (ذاتي) or accidental (عرضي) for the subject—this yields predication by essence or accident. Another division is by the type of relation: if the predicate relates to the subject via a preposition like 'fi' (in), 'dhu' (possessor of), or 'lahu' (having), it is derivative predication (بالاشتقاق), e.g., 'Zayd is standing' (Zayd has standing). If no mediation, it is equivalence predication (بالمُوَاطاة), e.g., 'Zayd is a human'. The author suggests the term 'predication' is homonymous between these two types. The passage then notes that every concept can be predicated of itself by primary predication, and thus self-negation is impossible. However, some universal concepts like 'concept' and 'possible' can also be predicated of themselves by common predication, while others like 'partial' cannot—instead their opposites are predicated. This leads to the requirement of unity of the mode of predication in contradiction, adding to the eight standard conditions. The passage ends by introducing a famous skeptical doubt: predication seems impossible because the concept of the subject (ج) is identical to the concept of the predicate? The text cuts off, but the resolution typically involves distinguishing different modes of consideration.

Key Terms
الحمل
الحمل الأولى
الحمل الشائع
الحمل بالاشتقاق
الحمل بالمُوَاطاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'النار حارة' (Fire is hot), which type of predication is being used according to the passage?
A) Primary predication (الحمل الأولى), because 'fire' and 'hot' are conceptually one.
B) Common predication (الحمل الشائع), because 'fire' and 'hot' are distinct concepts sharing existence.
C) Derivative predication (الحمل بالاشتقاق), because 'hot' is derived from 'heat'.
D) Equivalence predication (الحمل بالمُوَاطاة), because it is without preposition.

Key Takeaway
Predication has multiple modes; the most fundamental distinction is between primary (self-identity) and common (existential union), which underpins informative propositions in the sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Explain your reasoning.
?

Inclusive vs. Abstractive Predication
Author distinguishes two types of predication: inclusive and abstractive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between inclusive and abstractive predication and correctly classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Inclusive predication requires both relata; abstractive requires only the subject.

In the study of Islamic logic (mantiq), the way we predicate a property of a substance matters for understanding existence and attributes. This lesson unpacks the third subtlety (al-thalitha): the distinction between inclusive predication (al-insaf al-indimami) and abstractive predication (al-insaf al-intiza'i). The author clarifies when an attribute must exist in the external world and when it is merely a mental relation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relata
In any predication, there are two 'edges' (hashiyatan): the described (mawsuf) and the attribute (sifa). Locate them in the passage.
The described is the subject; the attribute is the quality or relation.
2
Check external actuality of attribute
For inclusive predication, both the subject and the attribute must be actual in the same external container. For abstractive, only the subject is actual; the attribute is a relation (nisba) that need not exist externally.
Example: 'The body is white' requires whiteness to exist in the body externally.
3
Apply the author's argument about necessity
The author argues that absolute attribution (thubut) is necessary even for abstractive predication. Something non-existent cannot be attributed to anything. So even a relation requires a real subject.
This ensures that predication is never purely fictional.
4
Classify examples
Use the criteria to classify: 'The body is white' is inclusive; 'The sky is above' is abstractive. Test other examples like 'Zayd is a father' or 'Fire is hot'.
Consider whether the attribute exists independently in the external world.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثَةُ : الإِنْصَافُ الإِنْضِمَامِيُّ يَسْتَدْعِي تَحَقَّقَ الْحَاشِيَتَيْنِ فِي ظَرْفِ الأَنْصَافِ بِخِلَافِ الاِنْتِزَاعِيُّ، يَسْتَدْعِي تُبُوتَ الْمَوْصُوفِ فَقَط ، فَمُطْلَقُ الإِنْصَافِ لَا يَسْتَدْعِي تُبُوتَ الصَّفَةِ في ظَرْفِهِ. أَمَّا مُطْلَقُ النُّبُوتِ فَضَرُورِيٌّ ; فَإِنَّ مَا لَا يَكُونَ مَوْجُودًا في نَفْسِهِ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ مَوْجُودًا لِشَيْءٍ وَالأَنْصَافُ لَيْسَ مُتَحَقِّفًا في الْخَارج ، حَتَّى يَلْزَمَ تَحَقُّقُ الصَّفَةِ فِيهِ; لِأَنَّهُ يَسْبَةٌ، وَكُلُّ نِسْبَةٍ تَحَقَّقُهَا فَرْعُ تَحَقَّقَ الْمُنتَسَبَيْنِ , بَلْ هُوَ مُتَحَفِّقٌ فِي الذِّهْنِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي الْإِنْضِمَامِيِّ الْخَارِجِيِّ الْمَوْصُوفِ مُتَحِدًا مَعَ الصِّفَةِ فِي الْأَعْيَانِ، كَالْجِسْمِ وَالْأَبْيَضِ، وَفِي الانْتِزَاعِيُّ الْخَارِجِي مُنْحِدًا بِحَسَبِ الْأَعْيَانِ , كَالسَّمَاءِ وَالْفَوْقِيَّةِ.
The third [subtlety]: The inclusive predication requires the actuality of the two edges in the container of the predication, unlike the abstractive, which requires only the establishment of the described. So absolute predication does not require the establishment of the attribute in its container. As for absolute attribution (thubut), it is necessary; for that which is not existent in itself cannot be existent for something. And predication is not actual in the external world so that it would require the actuality of the attribute in it; because it is a relation, and every relation's actuality is dependent on the actuality of the two relata. Rather, it is actual in the mind. And in the inclusive external, the described is united with the attribute in the individuals, like body and white. And in the abstractive external, it is united with respect to the individuals, like sky and above-ness.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines two types of predication (insaf): inclusive (indimami) and abstractive (intiza'i). Inclusive predication requires both the subject and the attribute to be actually present in the container (e.g., in the external world, both body and whiteness must exist as individuals). Abstractive predication requires only the subject to be actual; the attribute is a relation that does not need to exist separately. The author emphasizes that predication itself is not a real external thing but a relation that exists only in the mind. However, the foundation of attribution (thubut) is necessary: something non-existent cannot be attributed to anything. Examples: 'The body is white' is inclusive because whiteness inheres in the body externally; 'The sky is above' is abstractive because 'above-ness' is not an inherent attribute but a relation.

Key Terms
الإِنْصَاف الإِنْضِمَامِي
الإِنْصَاف الاِنْتِزَاعِي
الحَاشِيَتَان
النُّبُوت
نِسْبَة

A) 'The sky is above'
B) 'The body is white'
C) 'Zayd is a father'
D) 'The sun is risen'

Key Takeaway
Inclusive predication requires both relata to exist externally; abstractive predication only requires the subject, with the attribute as a mental relation.

Tamthil (Qiyas) in Usul al-Fiqh: Components and Methods
Defines tamthil and its fiqhi terminology, outlines methods for establishing the 'illa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of qiyas (asl, far', 'illa) and explain how dawaran and sabr wa taqsim function as methods to establish the 'illa.

Core Idea
Tamthil infers a ruling for a new case from an original case via a shared cause — the heart of Islamic legal analogy.

In this lesson, we explore tamthil, which Islamic jurists call qiyas. It is an inference from one particular case to another based on a common attribute. This is not the same as Aristotelian syllogism (qiyas in mantiq). Here, the jurists use specific terminology: the original case (asl), the new case (far'), and the effective cause ('illa). The passage also introduces two key methods for identifying the 'illa: dawaran (co-extensiveness) and sabr wa taqsim (scrutiny and division). Understanding these tools is essential for deriving legal rulings for new situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and structure of tamthil
Find the key phrase: 'التَّمْثِيلُ اسْتِدْلَالٌ بِجُزْئِيٍّ عَلَى جُزْئِيٍّ ; لِأَمْرٍ مُشْتَرَكِ'. Note the three components: the first particular (asl), the second particular (far'), and the shared matter ('illa).
The jurists call this qiyas, but be aware of the terminological overlap with mantiq.
2
Distinguish jurists' qiyas from logical qiyas
In mantiq, qiyas means syllogism (deductive inference from universals). Here, tamthil is analogical reasoning from particular to particular. Recognize the shift in meaning across disciplines.
The passage says 'وَالْفُقَهَاءُ يُسَمُّونَهُ قِيَاسًا'.
3
Understand dawaran (co-extensiveness)
Dawaran means that the ruling is present when the candidate 'illa is present, and absent when it is absent. This is expressed as tard wa 'aks. It is considered a strong sign ('aya) that the candidate is indeed the cause.
The passage says 'وَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانُ وُجُودًا وَعَدَمًا'.
4
Understand sabr wa taqsim (scrutiny and division)
This method enumerates possible attributes of the asl, eliminates those that are not suitable (by counter example or other criteria), leaving the valid 'illa. It yields probability (zann) not certainty.
The passage says 'وَهُوَ يُفِيدُ الظَّنَّ'.
5
Apply to a fiqh example: qiyas of nabidh to khamr
The asl is khamr (grape wine), the far' is nabidh (date wine), and the 'illa is intoxication. Using dawaran: wherever intoxicants are forbidden, intoxication is present; where not forbidden (e.g., non-intoxicating vinegar), intoxication is absent. This supports the 'illa.
This example is not in the text but is a typical usul al-fiqh illustration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّمْثِيلُ اسْتِدْلَالٌ بِجُزْئِيٍّ عَلَى جُزْئِيٍّ ; لِأَمْرٍ مُشْتَرَكِ ، وَالْفُقَهَاءُ يُسَمُّونَهُ قِيَاسًا ، وَالْأَوَّلَ أَصْلًا وَالثَّانِي فَرْعًا ، وَالْمُشْتَرَكَ عِلَّةً جَامِعَةً، وَلِإِثْبَاتِ الْعِلَّةِ طُرُقٌ، وَالْعُمْدَةُ الدَّوَرَانُ، وَيُعَبِّرُ عَنْهُ بِالطَّرْدِ وَالْعَكْسِ، وَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانُ وُجُودًا وَعَدَمًا ، قَالُوا الدَّوَرَانُ آيَةُ كَوْنِ الْمَدَارِ عِلَّةً لِلدَّائِرِ ، وَالتَّرْدِيدُ ، وَيُسَمَّى بِالسَّبْرِ وَالتَّقْسِيم، وَهُوَ تَتَبُّعُ الْأَوْصَافِ، وَإِبْطَالُ بَعْضِهَا؛ لِتَعَيُّنِ الْبَاقِي، وَهُوَ يُفِيدُ الظَّنَّ ، وَالتَّفْصِيلُ فِي أُصُولِ الْفِقْهِ .
Tamthil is an inference from a particular to a particular due to a shared matter. The jurists call it qiyas, calling the first (particular) the 'asl (original case) and the second the far' (branch), and the shared matter the 'illa (effective cause). For establishing the 'illa there are methods, the most important being dawaran (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness), which they refer to as tard wa 'aks (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness), and it is the conjunction in existence and non-existence. They said: dawaran is the sign that the maqif (circumstances) is the cause for the da'ir (the revolving thing). And al-tardid (alternation), also called sabr wa taqsim (scrutiny and division), which is the tracing of attributes and the nullification of some of them, so that the remainder becomes determined, and it yields probability (zann). The details are in usul al-fiqh.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines tamthil (analogical reasoning) and its terminology as used by Islamic jurists (fuqaha). Tamthil is an inference (istidlal) from a particular (juz'i) to another particular due to a shared attribute (amr mushtarak). The jurists call this qiyas, naming the first particular 'asl (original case) and the second far' (branch), and the shared attribute 'illa (cause). The author lists methods for establishing the 'illa: the most important is dawaran (rotation), which is expressed as tard wa 'aks—the presence and absence of the 'illa coincide with the presence and absence of the ruling. They say dawaran is a sign that the maqif (the circumstance) is the cause for the da'ir (the thing that revolves). Another method is tardid (alternation), also called sabr wa taqsim (scrutiny and division): one traces possible attributes, eliminates those that do not meet the conditions, and the remaining attribute is determined to be the 'illa. This method yields probability (zann), not certainty. The author notes that details are found in usul al-fiqh. No example is given here, but a typical fiqh example would be: qiyas of nabidh (date wine) to khamr (grape wine) in prohibition due to the shared 'illa of intoxication (tard wa 'aks).

Key Terms
تمثيل
قياس
علة
دوران

Checkpoint

A) Sabr wa taqsim
B) Dawaran
C) Tard wa 'aks
D) Both dawaran (expressed as tard wa 'aks)

Key Takeaway
Tamthil (qiyas) is a central tool in usul al-fiqh, relying on a shared 'illa established by methods like dawaran and sabr wa taqsim, producing probable knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas for a new situation: If cocaine is intoxicating, what are the asl, far', and 'illa? Then explain how dawaran would be used to verify the 'illa.
?

The Dispute on the Effect of Creation: Essence or Existence?
Author states the dispute on whether the effect of creation is the essence itself or its being described with existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two positions on the effect of creation (ja'l) and identify the key terms involved.

Core Idea
Is creation about the essence itself or its being painted with existence?

This passage introduces a fundamental debate in Islamic philosophy and theology: when something is created, what exactly is the effect? Is it the essence (mahiyya) itself, or is it the attribution of existence (wujud) to that essence? The distinction shapes how we understand divine action and the nature of contingent beings. Let's parse the two views and the key terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views
The passage states that philosophers and scholars differed. One view says the effect of creation is the mahiyya (essence) itself; the other says it is the ittisaf (being described) with wujud (existence). Note that the question is about 'athar al-ja'l (effect of creation).
2
Define the key terms
Ensure you understand 'al-ja'l' (the act of creation/bringing into being), 'al-mahiyya' (essence/quiddity), and 'al-wujud' (existence). In this context, existence is considered an attribute that can be added to the essence.
These terms are common in Kalam and falsafa debates.
3
Clarify the simple vs. composite distinction
The passage asks whether creation is simple (basiṭ) or composite (murakkab). 'Simple' would mean the effect is a single thing (e.g., the essence alone), while 'composite' would involve two aspects (e.g., essence plus existence). The dispute directly relates to this: if the effect is the essence itself, creation is simple; if it is the essence qualified with existence, creation is composite.
4
Summarize the disagreement in your own words
Write a sentence or two that captures the core issue: does God bring the essence itself into being, or does He bring the essence into being by attaching existence to it? Use the Arabic terms to reinforce learning.
This prepares you for deeper discussions in Kalam and falsafa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني من المقامين في كيفية الجعل، أهو بسيط أم مركب ؟ اختلف الحكماء والعلماء في أن أثر الجعل هو الماهية نفسها، أو اتصافها بالوجود؟
The second of the two topics concerning the mode of creation: is it simple or composite? The philosophers and scholars differed on whether the effect of creation is the essence itself, or its being described with existence?
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic: the mode of creation (ja'l). It asks whether creation is simple (basiṭ) or composite (murakkab). The actual dispute is defined: whether the effect of creation is the essence (mahiyya) itself or the attribution of existence to the essence. This is a fundamental question in Islamic philosophy and theology, affecting the understanding of how things come to be. The passage sets up the problem but does not provide the author's chosen answer here.

Refuting the Primacy of Existence: Three Ash'ari Arguments
Author refutes the doctrine that existence is the real entity and affirms essence as the existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three arguments used by the Ash'ari commentator to refute the doctrine that existence is the real entity, and explain why the essence itself is the true existent.

Core Idea
Existence is not a separate reality; the essence itself is the made and the existent.

The existence-essence debate is central to Islamic ontology. Some later philosophers argued that existence is the real entity, with essence as a mental abstraction. In this passage, the commentator presents three critical objections to this view from the Ash'ari perspective, defending the identity of essence and existence as held by al-Ash'ari and al-Suhrawardi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Read the beginning of the passage: 'some later philosophers held that truly the existent is existence, distinct from the essence.' Understand that the view being refuted posits existence as a separate reality.
2
Analyze the first refutation on al-maj'uliyya al-dhatiyya
The commentator argues that if 'essential made-ness' means the essence subsists for itself, it is not impossible; it is the core of the issue. He distinguishes simple making (al-ja'l al-basit) from composite making, showing that the made-ness of the referent is the essence itself.
Simple making does not require a separate made-to; it is the direct act of bringing the essence into existence.
3
Examine the second refutation on possibility
The opponent says the made per se is possible, and possibility is a quality of the relation. The commentator replies that the three modes (imkan, wujud, imtina') are qualities of the essence's self-determination, not relations.
This reframes possibility as intrinsic to the essence, not a relation to existence.
4
Understand the third refutation on otherness in making
The opponent demands otherness between the made and the maker. The commentator distinguishes simple vs composite making: only composite making requires otherness. Simple making does not even require a made-to, so the demand for otherness is irrelevant.
5
Conclude the correct position
The passage ends by affirming that the true existent is the essence, as held by al-Ash'ari and al-Suhrawardi. Summarize why the essence itself is the referent of existence and making.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيا: إن أريد بالمجعولية الذاتية ثبوت نفس الماهية لنفسها ففيه غفلة عن تصور معنى الجعل البسيط ، أو مجعولية مصداقه، وهو نفس الماهية، فلا استحالة فيه، بل هو أصل المسألة. ومن ههنا سقط ما قيل في إثبات لزوم المجعولية الذاتية أن حقيقتها مجعولة بثبوت الذات لنفسها باعتبار المصداق، ومصداقه ليس إلا نفس الماهية ، وقد قلتم إنها مجعولة. وثانيا إن المجعول بالذات ما هو ممكن بالذات، والإمكان كيفية النسبة. ويرد عليه أن المواد الثلاثة عندنا كيفيات نفس تقرر الماهية. وثالثا إن الجعل يستدعي مغايرة المجعول والمجعول إليه، ويرد عليه أن استدعاء المغايرة إنما هو في المؤلف، دون البسيط ؛ فإنه لا يستدعي مجعولا إليه ، فضلا عن المغايرة، والله أعلم. ثم اعلم أنه ذهب بعض المتأخرين إلى أن الموجود حقيقة هو الوجود مغايرا للماهية وهي متحدة معه نوع اتحاد، وموجودة بالعرض، والوجود متشخص بنفسه ، والمجعول بالذات هو الوجود جعلا بسيطا . وأورد عليه بيانات مطنبة، من أراد الاطلاع عليه(۱) فليرجع إلى كتبه، كـ «الأسفار» وحواشي «الشفاء» و«حكمة الإشراق». والحق الصحيح أن الموجود هو الماهية ، كما مرت الإشارة إليه آنفا، وإليه ذهب الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري قدس سره والشيخ المقتول (١).
Secondly: If by essential made-ness (al-maj'uliyya al-dhatiyya) is meant the subsistence of the essence itself for itself, then there is heedlessness of the conception of the meaning of simple making (al-ja'l al-basit), or the made-ness of its referent, which is the essence itself, so there is no impossibility in it; rather it is the origin of the issue. And from here falls what was said in proving the necessity of essential made-ness that its reality is made by the subsistence of the essence for itself in consideration of the referent, and its referent is nothing but the essence itself, and you have said that it is made. Secondly, that which is made per se (al-maj'ul bi al-dhat) is what is possible per se (mumkin bi al-dhat), and possibility (al-imkan) is a quality of the relation. And it is replied that the three modes (al-mawadd al-thalatha) with us are qualities of the self-determination of the essence. Thirdly, that making (al-ja'l) demands the otherness of the made and the made-to; and it is replied that demanding otherness is only in the composite (al-mu'allaf), not in the simple; for it does not demand a made-to, let alone otherness. And Allah knows best. Then know that some later [philosophers] held that truly the existent is existence (al-wujud), distinct from the essence, and they are united in some kind of union, and [the essence] exists accidentally, and existence is self-individuated, and the made per se is existence by simple making. And detailed proofs were brought against it; whoever wishes to study it should refer to his books such as al-Asfar, and commentaries on al-Shifa and Hikmat al-Ishraq. The true and correct view is that the existent is the essence, as was indicated earlier, and this is the view of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (may Allah sanctify his secret) and the martyred shaykh.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the doctrine that existence (al-wujud) is the real entity distinct from essence (al-mahiyya). The author, commenting on Sullam al-'Ulum, defends the Ash'ari position that the essence itself is the existent. The passage proceeds in several points. First, it examines the concept of 'essential made-ness' (al-maj'uliyya al-dhatiyya). If by that one means that the essence subsists for itself, then it is a misunderstanding of simple making (al-ja'l al-basit); the made-ness of the referent is the essence itself, so there is no impossibility. The author then notes that this refutes a previous argument about the necessity of essential made-ness. Second, the passage addresses the claim that the made per se is the possible per se, and that possibility is a quality of the relation. The author replies that in their school, the three modes (possibility, necessity, impossibility) are qualities of the self-determination of the essence, not of a relation. Third, it is argued that making requires otherness between the made and the made-to. The author responds that this requirement only applies to composite making, not to simple making, which does not even require a made-to, so otherness is irrelevant. The passage then mentions that some later philosophers (likely Mulla Sadra and the Illuminationists) held that existence is the real entity and essence is a mental construct. The author dismisses this view as incorrect, referring to detailed refutations in other works. Finally, the author affirms the true position: the existent is the essence, as held by al-Ash'ari and al-Suhrawardi (the martyred shaykh). Throughout, the commentary uses technical terms of Islamic philosophy: maj'ul (made), ja'l (making), mahiyya (essence), wujud (existence), imkan (possibility), etc. All examples and arguments are rooted in the kalam tradition, particularly the Ash'ari school.

Key Terms
المجعولية الذاتية
الجعل البسيط
الماهية
الوجود
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The relation between essence and existence
D) The divine act of creation

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari tradition holds that the essence itself is the existent; existence is not a separate metaphysical constituent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a simple argument in the style of this passage that defends the identity of essence and existence in the case of a created thing (e.g., a human being). Use the terms 'simple making' and 'essential made-ness'.
?
Identify which of the three refutations is most effective against the view that existence is a real addition to essence, and explain why using the passage's reasoning.

Self-Evidence of Knowledge: Aspect vs Essence
Author investigates self-evidence of knowledge and responses to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between self-evidence by aspect (bi-l-wajh) and self-evidence by essence (bi-l-kunh) and apply this distinction to evaluate epistemological disputes in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge is self-evident in concept, but its true essence remains a theoretical inquiry.

This passage delves into a central epistemological question: Is the concept of knowledge self-evident? The author affirms so, but a gloss raises objections and clarifies that 'self-evident' can mean two very different things. Understanding this distinction is vital for avoiding conflation between mere conceptual familiarity and genuine essential knowledge—a mistake that often underlies flawed theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of self-evidence
Find in the gloss where the author distinguishes between 'self-evident by aspect' (badihī bi-l-wajh) and 'self-evident by essence' (badihī bi-l-kunh). Note that the first is a superficial conceptual clarity, while the second is a deep grasp of the thing's true nature.
The Arabic terms are 'بالوجه' and 'بالكنه'.
2
Extract the inference from particular to universal
The gloss presents an argument: if knowledge of light (a particular) is self-evident, then knowledge in general (the universal) must be self-evident. Identify this inference and the two objections raised against it: (1) the universal must be essential (dhātī) to the particular, and (2) the particular must be known in its essence.
This is a form of immediate inference that the author evaluates.
3
Determine when the dispute is verbal
The author explains that if 'self-evidence' means by aspect, then the claim is necessary and the proof is complete; disagreement with those who deny it is merely verbal. If it means by essence, then the claim is theoretical and unproven. Apply this framework to the positions mentioned.
‘Verbal dispute’ (نزاع لفظي) is a key concept: the same words mean different things.
4
Apply to a similar theological example
Consider the claim 'the obligation of prayer is self-evident.' Using the distinction, analyze whether this refers to the concept (aspect) or the essence of obligation. What would the author say?
This step connects the logical tool to real Islamic reasoning.
