⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بديهي ، العلم بديعي وليعلم: أنهم اختلفوا في تصور العلم، فذهب جمع إلى أنه وجمع إلى أنه نظري ممكن التصورِ ، وجمع إلى أنه مأيوس، والمصنف اختار الأول، وقال: (وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ) ، أي العلم مِنْ أَجْلَى الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ، فإذن لا حاجة إلى المعرف إلا لفظا، ولذا استعمل فيه لفظ المدرك، (كَالنُّور وَالسُّرُور) ، أي كما أنهما بديهيان كذلك العلم، أو كالعلم بالنور والسرور . قال في «الحاشية»: «فيه إشارة إلى ما هو المشهور، من أن بديهة الخاص يستلزم بديهة العام ويرد عليه المنعان المشهوران: من أن ذلك إنما يتم لو كان العام ذاتيا للخاص، ويكون الخاص متصورا بالكنه، وكلاهما ممنوعان. ويمكن أن يقال: إن زيدا إذا كان معلوما بالضرورة، من حيث الكتابة، سواء كان معلوما بالكنه أو بالوجه كان بالضرورة مستلزما لبديهة معلوم الكتابة، فنقول العلم بالنور معلوم ممتاز من حيث العلم، الذي من لوازمه الامتياز عن الجهل به ، نعم، تنقیح حقيقته عسير ، وفيه ما فيه . اعلم: أن القائل بالبديهة إن أراد المعنى المصدري أو الحقيقي، وأراد بديهة التصور بالوجه فالدعوى ضرورية، والدليل عليه تام؛ لأن مفهوم العلم بالنور مقيد، وكما أن علمه مستلزم للعلم بالمطلق كذا ضرورته ضرورته ، لكن المنازعة حينئذ مع الجمعين الأخيرين لفظية. وإن أراد بديهة كنه العلم الحقيقي الذي هو مصداق هذا المفهوم فالدعوى نظرية، والدليل غير تام؛ لأنا لا نسلم أن علم النور معلوم بالكنه بالبديهة، إنما المعلوم المعنى المصدري. ثم اعلم أن النزاع في تصور الماهية النوعية للعلم وإدراكها ليس حضوريا، وإن كان إدراك جزئياته حضوريا، فلا يرد أن العلم صفة للنفس، وإدراكها بذاتها وصفاتها حضوري، فلا يتصف بالضرورة والنظرية . وما قيل إنه من مقولة الكيف ، فله جنس وفصل، فله حد، فتصوره تصور بالكنه، وهو من خصائص النظري ساقط ؛ لأن ذلك ممنوع، بل كما جاز كون تنبيه القضايا الضرورية في صورة الدليل كذلك يجوز كون معرف الضروريات التصورية في صورة الحد. ( نَعَمْ، تَنْقِيحُ حَقِيقَتِهِ)، أي حقيقة الإدراك الحقيقي تفصيلا ( عسير تنقيح حقيقة العلم عسير جدا ؛ لكمال ظهوره، بل خفائه أو اليأس عنه، ولذا اختلف فيه، فذهب علمائنا الماتريدية - عليهم الرحمة - إلى أنه حالة بسيطة قائمة بالمدرك ، ذات إضافة ، عليها مدار الامتياز. وقالوا: لو قام تلك الحالة بالجماد لكان شاعرا ، ومدار العلم والجهل على تعلقه بالواقعيات والاختراعيات، ولا يبالون بتعلق الإضافة بالمعدومات، ولا في كونها ممتازة امتيازا علميا، فتأمل فيه. وذهب الإمام الرازي - عليه الرحمة ـ إلى أنه إضافة، والعقل ينقبض عنه ، وذهب جمع إلى أنه مقارنة العالم للمعلوم في العقل الفعال، وهو باطل؛ جدا
Self-evident. Knowledge is self-evident. Know that they differed regarding the conceptualization of knowledge. A group held that it is [self-evident], another that it is theoretical and possible to conceptualize, and another that it is hopeless. The author chose the first and said: 'The truth is that it'—meaning knowledge—'is among the most evident of self-evident things.' So there is no need for a definition except verbally, and therefore the word 'percept' is used for it, like light and pleasure—just as both are self-evident, so is knowledge, or like knowledge of light and pleasure. He said in the Gloss: 'Therein is an indication of what is well-known, that the self-evidence of the particular necessitates the self-evidence of the universal, and it is refuted by the two well-known objections: that this is only complete if the universal is essential to the particular and the particular is conceptualized in its essence, both of which are denied. It can be said: If Zayd is known necessarily with respect to writing, whether known in essence or by aspect, he necessarily entails the self-evidence of the known writing. So we say: Knowledge of light is known as distinct with respect to knowledge, which has as a necessary concomitant distinction from ignorance of it. Yes, refining its reality is difficult, and there is what is in it.' Know: If the proponent of self-evidence intends the verbal or real meaning, and intends self-evidence of conceptualization by aspect, then the claim is necessary and the proof is complete; because the concept of knowledge of light is restricted, and just as his knowledge entails knowledge of the absolute, so its necessity entails its necessity. But the dispute then with the two latter groups is verbal. If he intends the self-evidence of the essence of true knowledge which is the referent of this concept, then the claim is theoretical and the proof is incomplete; because we do not grant that knowledge of light is known in essence by self-evidence—only the verbal meaning is known. Then know that the dispute regarding the conceptualization of the specific quiddity of knowledge and its apprehension is not presential, although the apprehension of its particulars is presential. So it is not refuted that knowledge is an attribute of the soul and its apprehension of its essence and attributes is presential, so it is not characterized by necessity and theory. And what is said that it belongs to the category of quality, so it has a genus and differentia, so it has a definition, so its conceptualization is conceptualization in essence, and that is among the properties of the theoretical—is invalid; because that is denied. Rather, just as it is permissible to present necessary propositions in the form of a proof, so it is permissible to present the definitions of necessary conceptualizations in the form of a definition. Yes, refining its reality—meaning the reality of real apprehension in detail—is difficult, extremely difficult, because of its complete evidentness, rather its hiddenness or despair of it. Hence they differed about it. Our Maturidi scholars—may mercy be upon them—held that it is a simple state subsisting in the perceiver, having a relation, upon which distinction depends. They said: If that state subsisted in an inanimate object, it would be sentient. And the basis of knowledge and ignorance is its connection to realities and inventions. They do not care about the relation to non-existents, nor about it being distinguished with a scientific distinction. So ponder it. Imam al-Razi—may mercy be upon him—held that it is a relation, and the intellect shrinks from it. A group held that it is the conjunction of the knower with the known in the active intellect, which is false; extremely so.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological investigation into the nature of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and whether its concept is self-evident (badīhī) or theoretical (naẓarī). The author begins by noting the disagreement: some say knowledge is self-evident, others say it is theoretical and definable, and others despair of defining it. The author (al-Mullā Ḥasan al-ʿAṭṭār, the commentator) sides with the first, affirming that knowledge is among the most evident of self-evident things. Therefore, any attempt to define it is merely verbal, not a real definition. He gives the example of light and pleasure, which are self-evident; similarly, knowledge is self-evident. Then he quotes a gloss (al-ḥāshiyah) that addresses a famous argument: if a particular (e.g., knowledge of light) is self-evident, then the universal (knowledge in general) must also be self-evident. Two objections are raised: (1) this inference works only if the universal is essential (dhātī) to the particular, and (2) only if the particular is known in its essence (bi-l-kunh). Both are denied. The gloss offers a rebuttal using the example of Zayd known necessarily as a writer—this implies the universal 'writer' is self-evident. Similarly, knowledge of light implies the concept of knowledge in general. The author then distinguishes two interpretations of 'self-evidence' (badīhah): if one intends the verbal/real meaning and self-evidence by aspect (bi-l-wajh), then the claim is necessary and proven, and dispute with those who deny it is merely verbal. If one intends the self-evidence of the essence (kunnh) of true knowledge, then the claim is theoretical and unproven, because we do not know the essence of knowledge by self-evidence—only the verbal meaning is known. The author further clarifies that the dispute about the specific quiddity (māhiyyah nawʿiyyah) of knowledge is not about presential knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḥuḍūrī), but about conceptualization (taṣawwur). Some object that since knowledge is a quality, it has a genus and differentia and thus a definition, so its conceptualization in essence is theoretical. The author rejects this, arguing that necessary conceptualizations can still be presented in definitional form, just as necessary propositions can be presented as proofs. The passage concludes by noting that refining the reality of knowledge is extremely difficult due to its evidentness or hiddenness. The author then reports three positions: the Māturīdī view that knowledge is a simple state with a relation (iḍāfah) and is the basis for distinction; al-Rāzī's view that it is pure relation (which the intellect recoils from); and the view that it is conjunction with the active intellect—this last is false.

Key Terms
العلم
البديهي
النظري
الكنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for the inference that 'self-evidence of a particular entails self-evidence of the universal' to be valid?
A) The particular must be known by essence and the universal must be accidental to the particular.
B) The universal must be essential (dhātī) to the particular and the particular must be known in its essence.
C) The particular must be known by aspect only.
D) The universal must be known by essence and the particular by aspect.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of a concept does not guarantee knowledge of its essence; distinguishing these two senses resolves many epistemological disputes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: The objection in the gloss claims that the inference from particular to universal is valid only if the universal is essential to the particular. Consider this statement: 'Since we know the obligation of prayer from revelation, the concept of obligation is self-evident.' Analyze: Is the universal 'obligation' essential to the particular 'obligation of prayer'? Use the author's criteria to evaluate the inference.
?
Task 2: Given the distinction between aspect and essence, evaluate this claim: 'The existence of the Creator is self-evident, therefore no proof is needed.' What would the author of this passage say? Support your answer with reference to the two meanings of 'self-evidence'.

The Quiddity of Knowledge: Three Theories
Author presents and evaluates three theories of knowledge's quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Māturīdī, Rāzī, and Active Intellect theories of the quiddity of knowledge and evaluate which qualifies as a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī).

Core Idea
The true quiddity of knowledge is elusive; three theories propose different essences, but only one withstands scrutiny.

After establishing that the concept of knowledge is self-evident, the author turns to its underlying reality. Here, we encounter three competing accounts from the Islamic theological tradition: the Māturīdī view that knowledge is a state with a relation, al-Rāzī's view that it is pure relation, and the view that it is conjunction with the active intellect. This lesson will dissect these positions and assess their strengths using the tools of definition analysis.

Guided Path
1
List the three positions on the quiddity of knowledge
Identify each theory mentioned: (1) Māturīdī: knowledge is a simple state (ḥālah) subsisting in the knower, possessing a relation (iḍāfah), which is the basis of distinction. (2) Al-Rāzī: knowledge is nothing but relation. (3) Others: knowledge is the conjunction (muqāranah) of the knower with the known in the active intellect.
The author labels the third as 'extremely false.'
2
Analyze the Māturīdī definition as a ḥadd or rasm
The Māturīdī description gives a genus (state) and a differentia (having a relation). Check if it includes all instances of knowledge and excludes non-knowledge. For example, does a relation alone (as in al-Rāzī) suffice? Is the state essential?
A hadd haqiqi requires genus and differentia that are essential.
3
Compare with al-Rāzī's theory
Al-Rāzī reduces knowledge to pure relation. Does this capture the full reality? The author says 'the intellect shrinks from it,' implying it is counterintuitive. Consider whether a relation can subsist on its own without a substrate.
The gloss earlier emphasized that knowledge is a state with a relation, not just relation.
4
Evaluate the Active Intellect theory
This theory makes knowledge dependent on an external principle (the active intellect). Identify why the author rejects it so strongly—likely because it contradicts the self-evident fact that knowledge is an attribute inherent in the knower.
The passage says 'bāṭil jiddan' (extremely false).

The Simple Concept: What Makes It Unacquirable?
Defines the simple concept as unacquirable due to lacking second movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the simple concept is not acquired and classify it as necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Simple concepts are directly known; they lack the second movement that defines acquisition.

This passage introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic logic: between simple and complex concepts, and between necessary and theoretical knowledge. The author defines the 'simple' (al-basīṭ) precisely by its lack of the second movement—the reasoning step that moves from principles back to the matter under investigation. Understanding this helps you see why some knowledge is immediate and needs no proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'البسيط' (the simple) in the Arabic. Notice that the author does not give a genus-differentia definition but a characterization by what it lacks.
The simple is defined negatively: it lacks the second movement.
2
Extract the defining feature
The text says 'لا يكون كاسبًا لفقدان الحركة الثانية' – it is not acquired because it lacks the second movement. This lack is the essential characteristic.
The 'second movement' is the movement from principles back to the requested matters.
3
Determine its knowledge category
The author later states that such intermediary cases (those lacking full movement) are actually necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge, because the definition of necessary knowledge applies to them, even if they are rare.
Necessary knowledge is immediate, not requiring acquisition.
4
Compare with theoretical knowledge
Theoretical knowledge requires arrangement and movement(s). The simple, lacking that movement, falls outside the theoretical category and into the necessary, despite not being listed among the six well-known divisions.
The six divisions are inductive, not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبَسِيطُ لَا يَكُونُ كَاسِبًا لفقدان الحركة الثانية، (فَلا بُدَّ مِنْ تَرْتِيبِ أُمُورِ لِلاكْتِسَابِ، وَهُوَ النَّظْرُ وَالْفِكْرُ). اعلم: أن البعض ذهبوا إلى أن المعتبر في النظري النظر بمعنى الحركتين: حركة من المطالب إلى المبادئ، وحركة من المبادئ إلى المطالب، والبعض إلى أن المعتبر الحركة الأولى، والمتأخرون إلى أن المعتبر لازم الحركة الثانية، وهو الترتيب. ويرد على الكل أنه يلزم الواسطة بين الضروري والنظري، وهي ما يحصل بإحدى الحركتين أو بالثانية فقط، أو بالأولى فقط؛ لعدم الاندراج في أقسام الضروري الست المشهورة، وعدم صدق تعريف النظري عليه. والجواب: أنها من الضروري؛ لصدق تعريفه عليه، وإنما لم يعد في الأقسام للندرة، والحصر استقرائي (٣)، وقد يعمم الحدس، وهو بعيد. ثم اعلم أن القول بكون الفكر حركة ليس حقيقيا، بل على المسامحة؛ لأن الحركة لا بد لها من فرد زماني تدريجي ينطبق على الزمان، ومن أفراد آنية غير متناهية متوهمة في آنات غير متناهية متوهمة في زمان الحركة، على ما تقرر في الطبيعيات، فلو كان الفكر حركة فإما في الصورة، وليست أفرادها
The simple (concept) is not acquired due to the lack of the second movement. Therefore, there must be an arrangement of matters for acquisition, and that is consideration (nazar) and thought (fikr). Know: Some have held that what is considered in the theoretical is consideration in the sense of two movements: a movement from the requested matters to the principles, and a movement from the principles to the requested matters. And some have held that what is considered is the first movement, and the later scholars have held that what is considered is the necessary consequence of the second movement, which is arrangement. An objection is raised against all: that an intermediary between the necessary and the theoretical is entailed, namely what is obtained by one of the two movements, or by the second alone, or by the first alone, because it is not included among the six well-known divisions of the necessary, and the definition of the theoretical does not apply to it. The answer: it is from the necessary because its definition applies to it; it was merely not counted among the divisions due to rarity, and the enumeration is inductive. And sometimes intuition (hads) is generalized, but that is far-fetched. Then know: The statement that thought is a movement is not literal, but rather metaphorical, because movement necessarily has a temporal gradual individual conforming to time, and from instantaneous individuals that are infinite and assumed in infinite instants assumed in the time of movement, as established in natural philosophy. So if thought were a movement, it would either be in the form, and its individuals are not...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage defines the 'simple' (basit) as a concept that cannot be acquired through reasoning because it lacks the second movement (i.e., the movement from principles back to the requested). The author then argues that acquisition requires an ordering of matters, which is what is meant by 'consideration' (nazar) and 'thought' (fikr). He presents differing views: some say theoretical consideration requires both movements, some say only the first (from requested to principles), and later scholars say it requires a necessary consequence of the second movement, namely arrangement. An objection is raised that these intermediate cases would fall outside the six well-known categories of necessary knowledge and also fail to match the definition of theoretical knowledge. The author replies that such cases are still necessary because the definition of necessary knowledge applies, but they are rare, so the enumeration is merely inductive (based on common occurrence). He also notes that some generalize intuition (hads) as a type, but this is far-fetched. Finally, he clarifies that calling thought a 'movement' is metaphorical, since physical movement requires temporal extension and continuous individuals, which thought does not have. This lesson guards against overly literal interpretations of technical terms and emphasizes the inductive nature of classifications.

Key Terms
البسيط
الحركة الثانية
الضروري
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is too complex to analyze
B) Because it lacks the second movement
C) Because it is based on intuition
D) Because it is part of the necessary categories

Key Takeaway
The simple concept is defined by its lack of the second movement, making it directly known and unacquired, thus belonging to necessary knowledge.

Thought as Movement: Metaphor Not Literal
Clarifies that calling thought a movement is metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that thought is only metaphorically called a movement and explain why.

Core Idea
Thought is not literal movement because movement requires temporal extension, which thought lacks.

This passage clarifies a common misconception in Islamic logic: the terms 'nazar' and 'fikr' (consideration and thought) are often described as movements, but this is metaphorical. The author provides a strict argument from natural philosophy: literal movement requires temporal gradual individuals, while thought is instantaneous. This lesson helps you avoid taking the metaphor literally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being made
The claim is that thought (fikr) is called a 'movement' (ḥaraka). Find where this is stated in the passage.
The author says: 'اعلم أن القول بكون الفكر حركة ليس حقيقيا'
2
Extract the definition of literal movement
The author defines what a genuine movement requires: a temporal gradual individual conforming to time, and infinite instantaneous individuals at infinite instants within the movement's time.
This is based on natural philosophy (al-ṭabīʿiyyāt).
3
Apply the definition to thought
Consider whether thought meets these conditions. Does it have temporal gradual individuals? The author implies it does not.
Thought occurs in an instant, not over a continuous time interval.
4
Conclude the metaphorical status
Since thought lacks the necessary features of literal movement, calling it a movement is a metaphorical expression (musāmaha).
The author says: 'ليس حقيقيا، بل على المسامحة'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How an Introduction Can Define a Science: Refuting Circularity
Author refutes the objection that an introduction cannot define a science due to circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection against the introduction defining a science and explain the author's refutation using the distinction between conceptual and assent knowledge.

Core Idea
A science's introduction defines it conceptually, without circularity, by separating conceptual from assent knowledge.

In studying any Islamic science, a critical question arises: can the introduction serve as the definition of that science? Some argue that this leads to circularity, since the introduction is studied before the problems of the science. In this passage, the author presents the objection and then refutes it with a nuanced distinction between two types of knowledge, showing how the introduction indeed can define the science without logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection's Three Reasons
Read the first part of the Arabic: the objectors claim the introduction cannot define the science because (a) the science's essence is its problems, which are not predicated, (b) definition depends on all problems causing circularity, and (c) the introduction would be outside the science.
These reasons rely on assuming that definition requires predication and that knowledge of problems is uniform.
2
Recognize the Rejection of a Weak Rebuttal
The author dismisses a weak response that mental parts can define without issue, because sciences are conventional, not real essences with unified external parts.
This shows the author distinguishes between real and conventional definitions.
3
Extract the Core Refutation: Two Types of Knowledge
The author's real answer: knowledge of the problems has two modes—conceptual ('ilm taṣawwurī) and assent ('ilm taṣdīqī). The introduction provides conceptual knowledge, which serves as a definition, while assent knowledge comes later. Thus, no circularity.
The introduction's content is only outside the assent portion, not the conceptual portion.
4
Analyze the Definition's Parts
The author concludes that definitions can be composed of mental parts (like concepts) or external parts. The introduction uses mental parts to define the science conceptually.
This broadens the notion of definition beyond mere external constituents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: قالوا: إن مقدمة الشروع لا يمكن أن يكون حدا للعلم؛ لأن حقيقة العلم مسائله وهي أجزاء غير محمولة، فلا يحد بها، ولأن حده ليست حدا موقوف على معرفة جميع تلك المسائل، فلو كان مقدمة لزم توقف الشروع في تلك المسائل على العلم بها، وهو دور ، ولأنه يلزم أن يكون المسائل خارجة عن العلم؛ لأن المقدمة خارجة عن ذلك العلم بيان نوعية أسامي الكتب والفنون وفيه نظر ـ لا لما قيل : إنه يجوز أن يكون للعلم أجزاء عقلية يحد بها ، ويتوقف عليه الشروع في المسائل، ويكون إدراكاتها خارجة عن العلم، فلا فساد ؛ لأن الأجزاء العقلية والخارجية إنما يكونان معا لماهيات حقيقية، والعلوم لتركبها من مقولات مختلفة ماهيتها اعتبارية، فأين يكون لها أجزاء متحدة في الوجود، وإلا لزم اتحاد المقولات ، بل ـ لأن العلم يتعلق بالمسائل بنحوين: علم تصوري وعلم تصديقي فالعلم بالمسائل بالنحو الأول، بل المسائل المعلومة بهذا النحو من العلم يكون حدا موقوفا عليه، والعلم بالنحو الثاني أو المعلوم بهذا النحو من العلم موقوفا، وخروج المقدمة ليس إلا من العلم التصديقي المتعلق بها، أو المعلوم به، والحد كما يكون من الأجزاء الذهنية كذا يكون من الأجزاء الخارجية جزاء الخارجية (٢) . هذا ، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Third: They said that the introduction to the science cannot be a definition of the science, because the essence of the science is its problems, and they are parts not predicated, so it is not defined by them; and because its definition depends on knowledge of all those problems, so if it were an introduction, then beginning those problems would depend on knowing them, which is circular; and because it would necessitate that the problems are outside the science, since the introduction is outside that science. Explanation of the type of names of books and arts. And in it there is a consideration – not because of what was said: that it is permissible for a science to have mental parts by which it is defined, and beginning the problems depends on it, and their perceptions are outside the science, so there is no corruption, because mental and external parts only occur together for real essences, while sciences, due to their composition from different categories, are conventional in essence, so how can they have parts unified in existence? Otherwise, the unification of categories would be necessary. But rather because knowledge relates to the problems in two ways: conceptual knowledge and assent knowledge. So knowledge of the problems in the first way, in fact the problems known by this way of knowledge, is a definition that depends on it, and knowledge in the second way or what is known by this way of knowledge is dependent. The exit of the introduction is only from the assent knowledge related to it, or what is known by it. And the definition, just as it is from mental parts, so too it is from external parts. This, and God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and its refutation. The objection claims that the introduction (muqaddimat al-shurū‘) of a science cannot serve as its definition (ḥadd), because the essence of the science is its problems (masā’il), which are parts not predicated (ajzā’ ghayr maḥmūla). Defining by such parts would cause circularity: understanding the definition would require prior knowledge of the problems, which are supposed to be learned later. Additionally, the introduction would be outside the science, making the problems external. The heading 'Explanation of the type of names of books and arts' precedes the refutation. The refutation first rejects a weak response (that mental parts can define without problem) because sciences are conventional, not real essences with unified parts. Then it offers the correct response: knowledge (al-‘ilm) relates to problems in two ways—conceptual (taṣawwurī) and assent (taṣdīqī). The introduction provides conceptual knowledge of the problems, which serves as a definition; the assent knowledge (the actual content of the science) comes later. Thus, the introduction's content is only external to the assent knowledge, not to the conceptual knowledge. A definition can be composed of either mental or external parts.

مقدمة الشروع
أجزاء عقلية
علم تصوري
علم تصديقي

C) By denying that the introduction is part of the science.
D) By claiming that circularity is permissible in definitions.

Key Takeaway
The introduction of a science defines it by providing conceptual knowledge of its problems, which is distinct from the assent knowledge that constitutes the science's content, thereby avoiding circularity.

The Four Demands: Conceptual vs Assented
Author classifies the four types of inquiry into conceptual and assented pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given demand (ma, ayyu, hal, lim) as either conceptual or assented, based on the classification in al-Sullam.

Core Idea
In logic, questions are of four types; two seek concepts, two seek judgments.

The author of al-Sullam distills all inquiry into four fundamental demands. Understanding which demand is used determines what kind of answer is required. This classification is the bedrock of logical investigation.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four demands
Learn the Arabic names: ma (ما), ayyu (أي), hal (هل), lim (لم). These are the ummahat (mothers) of demands.
The passage calls them 'ummahat al-matalib'.
2
Group by purpose
The passage explicitly pairs ma and ayyu as conceptual (tasawwuri) demands, and hal and lim as assented (tasdiqi) demands. Conceptual demands seek clarification of a concept; assented demands seek a judgment about truth.
3
Apply to real questions
Given a question from Islamic sciences, identify which demand it represents and whether it is conceptual or assented. For example, 'What is riba?' uses ma (conceptual); 'Is the world created?' uses hal (assented).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يطلب به أي ما يقع به الطلب التصور أو التصديق يسمى المطالب أربع مطلبا، وأمهات المطالب أربعة: ما، وأي، هما مطلبان تصوريان، وهل، ولم، وهما مطلبان تصديقيان. فما لطلب التصور بحسب شرح الاسم، من غير التفات إلى الوجود، سواء كان موجودا أم لا، فيجاب بالرسم والحد، من حيث إنه حد، بل من حيث إنه عنوان وشارح، فتسمى شارحة، أو لطلب التصور بحسب الحقيقة، من حيث إنها موجودة، فيجاب بالحد أي التام أو ما يقوم مقامه في البسائط الحقيقية، ومن أجاز الرسم في المركبات فقد خالف الشيخ الرئيس للصناعة، فحقيقية.
And that by which the request is made, i.e., that by which the request occurs, conceptual or assented, is called the demands: four demands. And the mothers of the demands are four: 'what' and 'which'—they are two conceptual demands; and 'whether' and 'why'—they are two assented demands. So 'what' is for requesting conceptualization according to explanation of the name, without regard to existence, whether it exists or not, so it is answered with description and definition, insofar as it is a definition, rather insofar as it is a title and explainer, so it is called 'explanatory'; or (it is) for requesting conceptualization according to the essence, insofar as it exists, so it is answered with the definition—the complete one—or what stands in its place in the simple realities, and whoever allows description in composites has contradicted the chief master concerning the craft, so it is 'essential'.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the four fundamental types of inquiry (matalib) in logic. It is taken from the matn of al-Sullam and is meant for memorization. The author states that the instrument by which inquiry is made—whether conceptual or assented—is called 'demands', and the primary ones (ummahat) are four: 'ma' (what is it?), 'ayyu' (which is it?/what kind?), 'hal' (is it?), and 'lim' (why is it?). The first two are conceptual demands, because they seek to clarify a concept; the last two are assented demands, because they seek to establish a judgment. The passage then elaborates on the first demand 'ma' (what?): it can be either for requesting conceptualization according to explanation of the name (sharh al-ism), disregarding actual existence—in which case the answer is either a description (rasm) or a definition (hadd) considered as an explanation—or for requesting conceptualization according to the essence (haqiqa), considering the thing as existent, in which case the answer is a complete definition (hadd tamm) or its equivalent for simple realities. The author notes that allowing description for composite things contradicts the position of the chief master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is, i.e., Avicenna) in the logical craft. Thus, the two types of answers are called 'shariha' (explanatory) and 'haqiqiyya' (essential).

Key Terms
مطالب
تصوري
تصديقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two demands are classified as 'conceptual' (tasawwuri)?

B) Ma and ayyu
C) Ma and hal
D) Ayyu and lim

Key Takeaway
The four demands are paired into conceptual (ma, ayyu) and assented (hal, lim), governing whether we seek meaning or judgment.

Two Senses of 'Ma': Sharh al-Ism vs Haqiqa
Author classifies the four types of inquiry into conceptual and assented pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of 'ma' demand – sharh al-ism (explanation of the name) and haqiqa (essential definition) – and identify the appropriate answer for each.

Core Idea
The same question 'What is it?' can ask for a name's meaning or a thing's essence.

The passage explains that the demand 'ma' (what is it?) has two distinct senses: one that asks for an explanation of the term without regard to existence, and one that asks for the real essence of an existing thing. Each sense requires a different type of answer.

Guided Path
1
Recognize 'ma' as conceptual
Recall that 'ma' is a conceptual demand. Now look within it: the passage says it can be for 'sharh al-ism' or for 'haqiqa'.
2
Define sharh al-ism
When 'ma' seeks explanation of the name, it disregards existence. The answer can be a description (rasm) or a definition (hadd) considered as an explanatory title. Example: 'What is Zakat?' answered as 'A specified portion of wealth given to the poor' (a description).
The passage calls this answer 'shariha' (explanatory).
3
Define haqiqa
When 'ma' seeks the essence (haqiqa), it regards the thing as existent. The answer must be a complete definition (hadd tamm) – genus and differentia – or its equivalent for simple realities. Description is not allowed for composites according to Avicenna.
The passage calls this answer 'haqiqiyya' (essential).
4
Apply the distinction
Given a question, decide if it asks for a name's meaning or a thing's essence. For example, a beginner asking 'What is salat?' likely wants sharh al-ism; a scholar asking 'What is the essence of salat?' wants haqiqa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يطلب به أي ما يقع به الطلب التصور أو التصديق يسمى المطالب أربع مطلبا، وأمهات المطالب أربعة: ما، وأي، هما مطلبان تصوريان، وهل، ولم، وهما مطلبان تصديقيان. فما لطلب التصور بحسب شرح الاسم، من غير التفات إلى الوجود، سواء كان موجودا أم لا، فيجاب بالرسم والحد، من حيث إنه حد، بل من حيث إنه عنوان وشارح، فتسمى شارحة، أو لطلب التصور بحسب الحقيقة، من حيث إنها موجودة، فيجاب بالحد أي التام أو ما يقوم مقامه في البسائط الحقيقية، ومن أجاز الرسم في المركبات فقد خالف الشيخ الرئيس للصناعة، فحقيقية.
And that by which the request is made, i.e., that by which the request occurs, conceptual or assented, is called the demands: four demands. And the mothers of the demands are four: 'what' and 'which'—they are two conceptual demands; and 'whether' and 'why'—they are two assented demands. So 'what' is for requesting conceptualization according to explanation of the name, without regard to existence, whether it exists or not, so it is answered with description and definition, insofar as it is a definition, rather insofar as it is a title and explainer, so it is called 'explanatory'; or (it is) for requesting conceptualization according to the essence, insofar as it exists, so it is answered with the definition—the complete one—or what stands in its place in the simple realities, and whoever allows description in composites has contradicted the chief master concerning the craft, so it is 'essential'.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification of the four fundamental types of inquiry (matalib) in logic. It is taken from the matn of al-Sullam and is meant for memorization. The author states that the instrument by which inquiry is made—whether conceptual or assented—is called 'demands', and the primary ones (ummahat) are four: 'ma' (what is it?), 'ayyu' (which is it?/what kind?), 'hal' (is it?), and 'lim' (why is it?). The first two are conceptual demands, because they seek to clarify a concept; the last two are assented demands, because they seek to establish a judgment. The passage then elaborates on the first demand 'ma' (what?): it can be either for requesting conceptualization according to explanation of the name (sharh al-ism), disregarding actual existence—in which case the answer is either a description (rasm) or a definition (hadd) considered as an explanation—or for requesting conceptualization according to the essence (haqiqa), considering the thing as existent, in which case the answer is a complete definition (hadd tamm) or its equivalent for simple realities. The author notes that allowing description for composite things contradicts the position of the chief master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is, i.e., Avicenna) in the logical craft. Thus, the two types of answers are called 'shariha' (explanatory) and 'haqiqiyya' (essential).

Key Terms
شرح الاسم
حد تام
رسم
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The 'ma' demand splits into two: one that accepts both definition and description as explanatory, and one that requires a complete definition for the essence.

Words Signify Mental Meanings, Not External Forms
Author argues that words signify meanings abstractly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's position on what words signify and explain the underlying disagreement between mental and external forms.

Core Idea
Words signify the meaning itself, not its mental or external image.

Words are the primary tool for conveying knowledge. But what exactly do they convey? Do they point to the mental concept in your mind, the physical object in the world, or the meaning abstracted from both? This passage from a classical logic text stakes out a clear position: words signify the meaning itself, stripped of any particular mode of existence. Understanding this is crucial for grasping how definitions and propositions work in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main thesis
Read the sentence beginning with 'وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ مَوْضُوعةٌ لِلْمعاني'. What does the author claim words are placed for? Note the qualification 'من حيثُ اللفظ موضوع بإزاء المعنى أي مع قطع النظر عن الوجودين'.
The author explicitly says words are for meanings irrespective of mental or external existence.
2
Contrast the two opposing views
Find the phrases 'كما ذهب إليه الشيخ' and 'كما قيل'. Who are the two opposing parties and what do they claim words signify? al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) holds that words signify mental forms; others say external forms.
The author rejects both extremes.
3
Analyze the underlying disagreement
The text says the disagreement is about what is known 'in itself' (المعلوم بالذات) or what is 'attended to' (الملتفت إليه). What does al-Shaykh believe is directly attended to? How does that lead to his view on signification?
Ibn Sina's position leads to judging universal meanings, not particulars.
4
Apply the distinction to an Islamic science example
Consider the term 'صلاة' (prayer). Does it signify the mental concept of prayer, the physical actions, or the meaning that applies to all valid prayers regardless of form? Based on the author's view, which one is correct?
This distinction is vital in usul al-fiqh when defining acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا كَانَ الْإِنْسَانُ مَدَنِي الطَّبع)، أي يَعْسُرُ معيشته إلا مع بني كَثِيرَ الافْتِقَارِ إِلَى التَّعْلِيم وَالتَّعَلّم، وكانت اللفظية الوضعية أعدها للمعاني ، (وَأَسْهَلَهَا) ؛ لكفايتها الغرض الذي هو العلم بالوضع، وعدم الحاجة إلى التدقيق في حصول الإفادة (فلها الاعتبار ) في العلوم. (وَمِنْ هَهُنَا تَبَيَّنَ أَنَّ الْأَلْفَاظَ مَوْضُوعةٌ لِلْمعاني، من حيثُ اللفظ موضوع بإزاء المعنى أي مع قطع النظر عن الوجودين؛ لأن غرض الواضع إفادة ما في الضمير، وليس المقصود في الإفادة إلا نفس الشيء، دون الصُّوَرُ الذَّهْنِيَّةِ)؛ كما ذهب إليه الشيخ، (أَوِ الصُّوَرُ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ ، كَمَا قِيل). قيل: مبنى الاختلاف على أن المعلوم بالذات ما هو ، وقيل : مبناه على أن الملتفت إليه ما هو ، فالشيخ أن ما هو حاصل في الذهن ملتفت إليه بالذات، وعليه مبنى كون الحكم زعم الجوامع المعاني مطلقا، لا الخصوصيات الذهنية أو الخارجية.
And since man is civil by nature, meaning his livelihood is difficult except with many people who are dependent on teaching and learning, and the verbal conventional [signs] are the most apt for meanings and the easiest, because they suffice for the purpose which is knowledge of the placement and lack of need for precision in obtaining understanding, so they are considered in the sciences. And from here it becomes clear that words are placed for meanings, in that the word is placed opposite the meaning irrespective of the two modes of existence [mental and external], because the purpose of the placer is to convey what is in the mind, and the intended in conveying is only the thing itself, not the mental forms as al-Shaykh held, nor the external forms as was said. It is said: the basis of disagreement is on what is known inherently, and it is said: its basis is on what is attended to inherently. So al-Shaykh holds that what is present in the mind is attended to inherently, and upon this is based the judgment that the sum of meanings is absolute, not mental or external particularities.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage discusses the purpose of verbal conventional signs. The author first notes that humans are social beings needing teaching and learning. Among signs, verbal conventional ones are the best and easiest because they fulfill the goal of conveying meaning without requiring deep analysis. Therefore, they are used in sciences. Then he argues that words are placed for meanings considered abstractly, regardless of whether the meaning exists in the mind or externally. The placer's aim is to inform of what is in the mind, so the conveyed object is the meaning itself, not the mental image (as some like al-Shaykh claim) nor the external object (as others claim). The underlying disagreement is whether the thing known in itself is the mental form or the external form. Al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) holds that the mental form is what is directly attended to, and thus judgments are made on the universal meaning, not on particular mental or external instances.

Words signify the meaning itself, not its mental representation or external reality.

Tadamun and Iltizam: No Necessary Entailment
Author explains why tadamuniyya and iltizamiyya do not necessitate each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish tadamuniyya from iltizamiyya and explain why neither necessitates the other.

Core Idea
Implicative and obligatory significations are independent; one can exist without the other.

This passage clarifies a fundamental distinction in Islamic logic: the implicative (tadamuniyya) and the obligatory (iltizamiyya) significations are not mutually entailing. Understanding this independence prevents errors when analyzing definitions and logical relationships in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of signification
From the key terms, define tadamuniyya (a word indicates part of its meaning, e.g., 'house' implies a room) and iltizamiyya (a word indicates an external concomitant, e.g., 'roof' implies a wall).
2
Extract the author's claim
The author states: 'There is no necessary entailment between them.' That is, tadamun does not imply iltizam, nor does iltizam imply tadamun.
3
Analyze why iltizam does not necessitate tadamun
The author says it is 'apparent' because iltizam occurs in 'simples' (basa'it) as well. Simples have no parts, so they cannot have tadamun (which requires parts). Thus, iltizam can exist without tadamun.
4
Analyze why tadamun does not necessitate iltizam (the problematic part)
The author notes this is problematic on the Imam's view if necessity is taken as estimation. Al-Sayyid critiques the notion that 'negation of the other' is a mental concomitant for every meaning, arguing we often conceive meanings without awareness of negations.
5
Understand al-Sayyid's resolution
Al-Sayyid distinguishes between a general clear concomitant (conception of implied plus concomitant suffices for judgment) and a specific clear concomitant (conception alone necessitates the concomitant). Iltizam requires the specific sense, which tadamun does not provide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهمه فهمه عقلا أو عرفا (۱) ، (وَأَمَّا التَضَمَّنيَّةُ وَالالْتِزامِيَةً فَلَا لُزُومَ بَيْنَهُمَا ) . عدم استلزام الالتزام إياه ظاهر؛ لتحققه في البسائط أيضا، وأما عدم استلزام التضمن إياه فمشكل على مذهبه على تقدير أخذ اللزوم التقديري. قال السيد في حواشي الشمسية (۱۸۷/۱، ۱۸۸): مبناه - أي قول الإمام ـ على أن سلب الغير لازم ذهني لكل معنى من المعاني، بحيث يلزم من حصوله في الذهن حصوله فيه، وليس بصحيح؛ فإنا نتصور كثيرا من المعاني مع الغفلة عن سلب غيرها عنها، ولو لاستلزم كل تصور تصديقا، وهو باطل قطعا . نعم، سلب الغير لازم بين بالمعنى الأعم، وهو أن يكون تصور الملزوم مع تصور اللازم كافيا في الجزم بينهما باللزوم، واللزوم المعتبر في الالتزام هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص، وهو أن يكون تصور الملزوم مستلزما لتصور اللازم . وانظر أيضا السعدية: ١٢٣ .
He understood it rationally or conventionally. As for the implicative and the obligatory, there is no necessary entailment between them. The non-necessity of the obligatory is apparent, because it occurs in simples as well. As for the non-necessity of the implicative, it is problematic on his view if the necessity is taken as estimation. Al-Sayyid said in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya (1/187,188): Its basis — i.e., the Imam's statement — is that the negation of the other is a mental necessary concomitant for every meaning, such that from its occurring in the mind its occurring in it is necessary. But this is not correct, for we conceive many meanings while being heedless of the negation of others from them, and if every conception necessitated a judgment, that is absolutely false. Yes, the negation of the other is a clear necessary concomitant in the general sense, which is that the conception of the implied together with the conception of the concomitant suffices for the judgment of necessity between them. The necessity considered in the obligatory is the clear necessary concomitant in the specific sense, which is that the conception of the implied necessitates the conception of the concomitant. Also see al-Sa'diyya: 123.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between two types of significance: the implicative (tadamuniyya) and the obligatory (iltizamiyya). The author states that there is no necessary entailment between them. The non-necessity of the obligatory is obvious because it can be found in simple concepts as well, whereas the non-necessity of the implicative is problematic on the Imam's view if one takes necessity as estimation. The commentator then quotes al-Sayyid from his glosses on al-Shamsiyya, who examines the basis of the Imam's statement that the negation of the other is a mental concomitant for every meaning. Al-Sayyid argues this is incorrect because we often conceive meanings without being aware of their negations of others; otherwise every conception would entail a judgment, which is false. However, the negation of the other is a clear concomitant in a general sense (where conception of the implied together with the concomitant suffices for judging necessity), but the necessity considered in obligatory implication is a clear concomitant in a specific sense (where the conception of the implied alone necessitates the conception of the concomitant). Thus, the passage clarifies the distinction between tadamun and iltizam and the conditions under which they are necessary.

Key Terms
التَضَمَّنيَّة
الالْتِزامِيَة
اللُّزُوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it occurs in compound concepts only

D) Because it is a clear concomitant

Key Takeaway
Tadamun and iltizam are independent types of signification; iltizam requires a specific clear concomitant that tadamun does not provide.

Words: Individual vs Compound – The Primary Criterion
Author defines individual and compound words focusing on the word as the subject of the attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the criterion of intended indication to determine whether a given word is individual or compound.

Core Idea
A word is compound only if its parts intentionally indicate parts of its meaning – proper names are a tricky exception.

This lesson focuses on the primary definition laid down by the author: individuality and composition are attributes of the word itself. You will learn to apply the key condition (intended indication of parts) to distinguish between the two, using the classic example of 'the rational animal' as a proper name versus a description.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its parts
Take the word in question and list its meaningful parts (e.g., roots, affixes, or separate words if it's a phrase).
2
Determine if parts correspond to meaning parts
Check whether each part of the word corresponds to a distinct part of the meaning. For example, in 'animal', the meaning ['living', 'sentient'] is not indicated by 'an-' and '-imal' separately – but in 'rational animal' as a description, 'rational' and 'animal' each contribute a part.
3
Check if the indication is intended (maqsuda)
Ask: Is the part of the word deliberately meant to indicate that specific part of the meaning? In proper names, even if the word has etymological parts, they are not intended to indicate the current meaning (e.g., 'Abdullah' as a proper name does not intend to indicate the servant of Allah).
The source emphasizes that the indication must be intended (dalala maqsuda). Accidental or historical indication does not count.
4
Apply the condition
If both conditions hold (parts of word correspond to parts of meaning with intended indication), the word is compound (مُرَكَّب). Otherwise, it is individual (مُفْرَد).
The author notes that individuality and composition are essentially attributes of the word, not the meaning. Meaning is described as individual/compound only by extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَبْحَثُ الإِفْرَادِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ الْإِفْرَادُ وَالتَّرْكِيبُ حَقِيقَةً) وبالذات (صِفَةُ اللَّفْظِ)، والمعنى إنما يوصف المركب والمفرد بهما بالتبعية (۳)، (لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ دلالة مقصودة (٢) ، فخرج مثل «الحيوان الناطق» عَلَمًا . قاله السيد في حواشي الشمسية (۲۳۲/۱) ، ثم قال : فيقال: المعنى المفرد ما يستفاد من اللفظ المفرد والمعنى المركب ما يستفاد من اللفظ المركب، وبعبارة أخرى: المعنى المركب ما يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه، والمعنى المفرد ما لا يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه ، سواء كان هناك للمعنى واللفظ جزء أو لا يكون لشيء منهما جزء، أو يكون لأحدهما جزء دون الآخر. (۲) كذا في السلم وفي شرح بحر العلوم: إن دل جزء اللفظ ...» وما في السلم أولى.
Section on Individual and Compound. Individual and compound, in reality and essentially, are attributes of the word. Meaning is described as compound or individual only secondarily. Because if its part indicates part of its meaning with intended indication, then it is compound; otherwise it is individual. For example, 'the rational animal' as a proper name is excluded. Al-Sayyid said in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya: Then it is said: The individual meaning is what is understood from the individual word, and the compound meaning is what is understood from the compound word. In other words: The compound meaning is that whose part is understood from part of its word, and the individual meaning is that whose part is not understood from part of its word, whether the meaning and word have parts or not, or one has a part and the other does not. It is like this in al-Sullam and in Sharh Bahr al-Ulum: 'if a part of the word indicates...' and what is in al-Sullam is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of 'individual' (ifrad) and 'compound' (tarkib) as applied to words and meanings. The author states that individuality and composition are in reality and essentially attributes of the word itself, not of the meaning. The meaning is only described as individual or compound secondarily, based on how the word signifies it. The condition for a compound word is that a part of the word indicates a part of the meaning with intended indication. Conversely, an individual word is one where this condition is not met. For example, 'the rational animal' when used as a proper name (e.g., a name for a person) is considered individual because the parts of the word do not indicate parts of the intended meaning (the person) in that usage. The commentator expands by quoting al-Sayyid, who clarifies that an individual meaning is something understood from an individual word, while a compound meaning is understood from a compound word. Furthermore, a compound meaning is one whose part is understood from a part of its word, whereas an individual meaning's parts are not understood from parts of the word. This distinction holds regardless of whether the meaning or word actually has parts. The textual note at the end suggests a variation in the wording of the condition between al-Sullam and the commentary.

Key Terms
الإفراد
التركيب
دلالة مقصودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it does not have parts
B) Because its parts do not indicate parts of the meaning with intended indication
C) Because it is a proper name, which is always individual
D) Because it is a description, not a definite term

Key Takeaway
The distinction between individual and compound words hinges on whether the word’s parts intentionally indicate parts of its meaning – context and usage are decisive, not the mere presence of parts.

Deriving Individual and Compound Meanings from Words
Author extends the definition to meanings, quoting al-Sayyid on individual and compound meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the definition of individual and compound meanings from the corresponding word definitions, and explain the cases where meaning or word may lack parts.

Core Idea
Individual and compound meanings are secondary – they are defined based on how the word's parts relate to the meaning's parts, even if some parts are absent.

In this lesson, we explore the extension of the distinction to meanings, as elaborated by al-Sayyid al-Jurjani. The author quotes him to clarify that an individual meaning is understood from an individual word, and a compound meaning from a compound word. More precisely, a compound meaning is one whose part is understood from a part of its word, whereas for an individual meaning this is not the case, regardless of whether the meaning or word actually has parts. This nuance is crucial for rigorous conceptual analysis in logic and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the secondary nature of meaning attributes
Recall that individuality and composition are primarily attributes of the word. Meaning is described as individual or compound only by extension (bil-taba'iyya). This means we can talk about 'compound meaning' because the word expressing it is compound.
2
Recall al-Sayyid's first formulation
Al-Sayyid states: 'The individual meaning is what is understood from the individual word, and the compound meaning is what is understood from the compound word.' This relates meanings directly to the words that convey them.
3
Analyze the alternative formulation
The second formulation provides an operational test: a compound meaning is one whose part (of meaning) is understood from a part of its word. An individual meaning is one whose part is not understood from a part of its word. This does not require the meaning or word to actually have parts; it is about the relationship of comprehension.
For example, the meaning of 'Zayd' (a particular person) has parts (e.g., height, color) but these are not understood from parts of the name 'Zayd' – so it is an individual meaning.
4
Consider the exceptional cases
The passage explicitly covers cases: whether both meaning and word have parts, one has parts, or neither has parts. The definition holds because it is based on whether a part of the meaning is understood from a part of the word, not on the existence of parts.
This prevents confusion that might arise from thinking that a word without parts cannot express a composite meaning (e.g., a simple word like 'جبل' can refer to a mountain which has parts, but the word is individual because its parts don't indicate those meaning parts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَبْحَثُ الإِفْرَادِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ الْإِفْرَادُ وَالتَّرْكِيبُ حَقِيقَةً) وبالذات (صِفَةُ اللَّفْظِ)، والمعنى إنما يوصف المركب والمفرد بهما بالتبعية (۳)، (لِأَنَّهُ إِنْ دَلَّ جُزْؤُهُ عَلَى جُزْءِ مَعْنَاهُ دلالة مقصودة (٢) ، فخرج مثل «الحيوان الناطق» عَلَمًا . قاله السيد في حواشي الشمسية (۲۳۲/۱) ، ثم قال : فيقال: المعنى المفرد ما يستفاد من اللفظ المفرد والمعنى المركب ما يستفاد من اللفظ المركب، وبعبارة أخرى: المعنى المركب ما يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه، والمعنى المفرد ما لا يستفاد جزؤه من جزء لفظه ، سواء كان هناك للمعنى واللفظ جزء أو لا يكون لشيء منهما جزء، أو يكون لأحدهما جزء دون الآخر. (۲) كذا في السلم وفي شرح بحر العلوم: إن دل جزء اللفظ ...» وما في السلم أولى.
Section on Individual and Compound. Individual and compound, in reality and essentially, are attributes of the word. Meaning is described as compound or individual only secondarily. Because if its part indicates part of its meaning with intended indication, then it is compound; otherwise it is individual. For example, 'the rational animal' as a proper name is excluded. Al-Sayyid said in his glosses on al-Shamsiyya: Then it is said: The individual meaning is what is understood from the individual word, and the compound meaning is what is understood from the compound word. In other words: The compound meaning is that whose part is understood from part of its word, and the individual meaning is that whose part is not understood from part of its word, whether the meaning and word have parts or not, or one has a part and the other does not. It is like this in al-Sullam and in Sharh Bahr al-Ulum: 'if a part of the word indicates...' and what is in al-Sullam is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of 'individual' (ifrad) and 'compound' (tarkib) as applied to words and meanings. The author states that individuality and composition are in reality and essentially attributes of the word itself, not of the meaning. The meaning is only described as individual or compound secondarily, based on how the word signifies it. The condition for a compound word is that a part of the word indicates a part of the meaning with intended indication. Conversely, an individual word is one where this condition is not met. For example, 'the rational animal' when used as a proper name (e.g., a name for a person) is considered individual because the parts of the word do not indicate parts of the intended meaning (the person) in that usage. The commentator expands by quoting al-Sayyid, who clarifies that an individual meaning is something understood from an individual word, while a compound meaning is understood from a compound word. Furthermore, a compound meaning is one whose part is understood from a part of its word, whereas an individual meaning's parts are not understood from parts of the word. This distinction holds regardless of whether the meaning or word actually has parts. The textual note at the end suggests a variation in the wording of the condition between al-Sullam and the commentary.

Existential Words as Incomplete Verbs
Author defines kalimat wujudiyya as incomplete verbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify existential words (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya) and explain why they are classified as incomplete verbs in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Existential words are relational connectors, not independent predicates.

In this passage, the author defines a special class of words called 'existential words' (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya). These are not ordinary verbs that convey full actions; rather, they are incomplete verbs like kana which indicate a relation between something and something else, along with time. Understanding this distinction is crucial for logical analysis of propositions in Islamic sciences, because the copula (nisba) expressed by these words determines the logical form of judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate 'al-kalimat al-wujudiyya' in the Arabic passage. This is the term under definition.
2
Extract the genus
The author says they are 'incomplete verbs' (al-af'al al-naqisa). So the broader category is verbs, but a special subset.
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes them? They indicate the being of something as something not yet mentioned (kawn al-shay'i shay'an lam yudhkar ba'du). Also they indicate time through their stipulated forms.
4
Understand the relational function
These words serve as copula, linking subject to predicate, not as independent predicates. For example, in 'Zayd was standing', 'kana' links Zayd to standing.
The copula is crucial for logical propositions.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the Quranic verse 'وكان الله غفورا رحيما'. The word 'kana' is an existential word indicating the relation of Allah to forgiveness and mercy, with past time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ. الكلمات. والحق أن الكلمات الوجودية هي الأفعال الناقصة، فإن كان مثلها معناه كون الشيء شيئا لم يذكر بعد. وإن لم يكن معناه مرآة لتعرف حال الغير، فإن دل بهيئة وضعية على الزمان، بأن يكون كل هيئة كذا واقعة في مادة متصرفة تدل على زمان كذا، وأوضاع الكلم للزمان كذلك على ما حكم به أهل العربية. فكلمة. اعلم أن الكلمة تدل على معنى واحد إجمالي مستقل، يحلله العقل إلى الحدث والزمان والنسبة العديمي الاستقلال، على ما يشهد به الوجدان، وأيضا وقوعها محكوما بها دائما يدل عليه وما قيل إنها محكوم بها باعتبار المعنى التضمني غير سديد؛ لاستلزامه وجود المجاز بلا حقيقة شائعا، فتأمل فيه. وأما عدم وقوعها محكوما عليها فللاشتمال على نسبة مقتضية للحكم بها على الغير. هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
Chapter on words. Words. The truth is that existential words are incomplete verbs. For example, 'kana' means the being of something as something not yet mentioned. And if its meaning is not a mirror to know the state of another, then if it indicates by a stipulated form the time, such that each such form occurs in a flexional matter indicating a time, and the forms of words for time are likewise according to what the Arabic grammarians have ruled, then it is a word. Know that the word indicates a single summary independent meaning, which the mind analyzes into the event, time, and relation that lack independence, as intuition witnesses. Also its always being predicated indicates that. And what has been said that it is predicated according to its implied meaning is not sound, because it entails common metaphor without a literal meaning, so reflect on it. As for its not being subject, it is because it includes a relation necessitating its predication upon another. This, and God knows best the correct.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage defines existential words (al-kalimat al-wujudiyya) as incomplete verbs (al-af'al al-naqisa), such as 'kana' and its sisters. The author explains that these words do not indicate a complete state of affairs; rather, they signify a relation between something and something else not yet specified. They indicate time through their stipulated forms, according to Arabic grammar. Then the passage clarifies that the word (kalima) denotes a single, independent meaning that the mind analyzes into three components: the event (hadath), the time (zaman), and the relation (nisba). These components are not independent themselves. The author argues that a word always serves as predicate (mahkum biha), never as subject, because it inherently contains a relation that requires being attributed to something else. This refutes an alternative view that the word is predicated in an implied sense, which would lead to widespread metaphor without literal meaning.

Key Takeaway

The Three Components of a Word and Its Predicative Status
Author analyzes the word (kalima) into event, time, and relation, and asserts it is always predicated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a word (kalima) into its three logical components (event, time, relation) and explain why it is always predicated.

Core Idea
Every word reduces to event, time, and a relation that cannot stand alone.

The passage then turns to the word (al-kalima) itself, explaining that even though it indicates a single meaning, the mind can analyze it into three elements: the event (al-hadath), the time (al-zaman), and the relation (al-nisba). Crucially, the word always serves as a predicate, never as a subject, because it inherently contains a relation that requires being attributed to something else. This analysis is foundational for understanding how propositions are formed in Islamic logic and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the word
The author states that a word indicates a single summary independent meaning (معنى واحد إجمالي مستقل). Locate this in the passage.
2
Analyze the word into components
The mind analyzes this meaning into the event (al-hadath), time (al-zaman), and relation (al-nisba). These components lack independence individually.
For example, the verb 'ضرب' (hit) contains the event of hitting, past time, and a relation that must be tied to a subject.
3
Understand the predicative status
The author argues that the word always occurs as a predicate (mahkum biha), never as a subject, because it contains a relation necessitating predication on another.
The passage refutes the alternative view that it is predicated by implied meaning, as that would lead to widespread metaphor without literal meaning.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the verb 'يقرأ' (yaqra'u - he reads) from the Quranic verse 'اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ' (Read in the name of your Lord). The verb contains the event of reading, present time, and a relation that connects the reading to an implied subject 'you'.

Distinguishing Arabic Verb from Logical Word
Author explains that not every Arabic verb is a logical word due to truth-bearing capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether an Arabic verb is a logical word (kalima) or a complete proposition based on truth-bearing capacity.

Core Idea
An Arabic present tense verb is a proposition, not a logical word, because it can be true or false.

In Arabic grammar, a verb like 'amshi' (I walk) is a single word (fi'l). But logicians treat it as a complete proposition because it contains a subject, predicate, and tense, allowing truth evaluation. This lesson explains the distinction and its importance for logical analysis of Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Define the grammatical verb (fi'l)
In Arabic grammar, fi'l is a word that indicates an event tied to a time. Examples: 'amshi' (I walk) and 'tamshi' (you walk) are verbs.
These are present tense verbs with attached pronouns indicating the subject.
2
Define the logical word (kalima)
In logic, kalima is a simple word that does not by itself constitute a proposition and cannot be true or false. It requires combination with another word to form a complete meaning.
For example, the noun 'insan' (human) alone is not true or false.
3
Apply the truth-bearing test
Check whether the expression can be evaluated as true or false. If it can, it is a proposition (qadiyya), not a simple word. 'Amshi' can be true (if I am walking) or false (if I am not), so it is a proposition.
This is the key criterion given in the passage.
4
Analyze the logical structure
For a singular proposition like 'amshi', two expressions are needed: one for the subject (indicated by the prefix hamza) and one for the predicate (the past tense material 'mashaytu'). The logician breaks the verb into its components.
The author mentions hamza and past tense material as indicators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كل فعل عند العرب أي النحاة كلمة عند المنطقيين؛ فإن الفعل العربي نحو أمشي وتمشي مثلا، أي المضارع المتكلم والحاضر فعل عندهم، وليس بكلمة عند المنطقيين، لاحتماله الصدق والكذب. وإذا بطل قضية أحادية لا بد من لفظين دالين على المسند والمسند إليه، وليس إلا الهمزة وأمثالها ومادة الماضي وهما صالحان للدلالة عليها، فحكمنا بها.
And not every verb according to the Arabs (i.e., the grammarians) is a word according to the logicians. For the Arabic verb such as 'amshi' and 'tamshi' for example, i.e., the first and second person present tense, is a verb according to them, but is not a word according to the logicians, because it possibly indicates truth and falsehood. And if a singular proposition fails, two expressions indicating the subject and predicate are necessary, and these are only the hamza and its likes and the past tense material, and both are suitable to indicate them, so we judge accordingly.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the Arabic grammatical verb (fi'l) and the logical word (kalima). The author states that not every verb in Arabic is considered a word in logic. For example, 'amshi' (I walk) and 'tamshi' (you walk) are verbs according to grammarians, but logicians do not count them as words because they can be true or false. In logical analysis, a simple word cannot bear truth value; only a complete proposition (qadiyya) does. Since these verb forms contain a subject, predicate, and time, they form a full proposition. Thus, for a singular proposition like 'I walk', two expressions are needed: one for the subject (the doer indicated by the prefix) and one for the predicate (the action). The prefix hamza and the past tense material serve these roles.

Key Takeaway

The Noun's Property: Judgment on Meaning vs. Sound
Defines noun and its judgment property, rebuts a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between judgment on meaning and judgment on sound, and explain why statements like 'daraba is a verb' do not refute the noun's property.

Core Idea
The noun's unique property is judgment on meaning; judgment on sound applies to any word.

In this passage, the author defines the noun and highlights its distinctive property: judgment upon its meaning. A potential objection arises from statements like 'daraba is a verb' that seem to judge a non-noun. The commentator clarifies that such judgments target the sound, not the meaning, and thus do not undermine the noun's specific property. By carefully distinguishing between judging the utterance itself and judging its conceptual content, we gain precision in logical-grammatical analysis.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the definition of the noun
The passage states that if a word's form does not indicate time, it is a noun. This is the defining condition. Note that time indication is a property of verbs and particles; its absence indicates a noun.
2
2. Extract the two types of judgment
The author distinguishes judgment on the sound of a word (e.g., 'daraba is a past tense verb' judges the utterance) from judgment on its meaning (e.g., 'Zaid is standing' judges the meaning of 'Zaid'). The former applies to any word, even meaningless ones; the latter is specific to nouns.
3
3. Apply the distinction to the counterexample
Consider the objection: 'daraba is a verb' is a judgment about a verb, not a noun. The commentator replies that this is a judgment on the sound (lafz) of 'daraba', not on its meaning. Therefore, it does not violate the rule that judgment on meaning is a property of nouns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِلَّا) ، أي وإن لم يكن بهيئته دالا على الزمان (فَهُوَ اسْمٌ، وَمِنْ خواصه عليه الْحُكْمُ عَلَيْهِ، وَقَوْلُهُمْ مِنْ حَرْفٌ جَرَّ وضَرَبَ فِعْلٌ مَاضٍ لَا يَرِدُ عَلَيْهِ » نَقْضَا ] (١) ، فَإِنَّهُ حُكْمٌ عَلَى نَفْسِ الصَّوْتِ)، أي صوت «من» و«ضرب»، (لا عَلَى مَعْنَاهُ)، أي معنى «من» و «ضرب»، (وَالْمُخْتَصُّ بِهِ)، أي بالاسم (هُوَ هَذَا ، أي الحكم على معناه ؛ إذ يوجد فيه ولا يوجد في غيره، لكن لا مطلقا، بل إذا لم يتصور بعنوان مفهوم اسمي جعل مرآة لملاحظته، وأما إذا جعل عنوانه مفهوما اسميا فيحكم عليه، فلا يرد معنى من غير مستقل، ولا يحتاج إلى هذا العذر الحاكم على الطبيعة في المحصورات أو ما في قوتها، بل الحاكم على الأفراد، (وَالْأَوَّلُ)، أي الحكم على نفس الصوت (يَجْرِي في الْمُهْمَلَاتِ أَيْضًا نَحْوُ جسق مُهْمَلٌ ] (٢)
Otherwise, i.e., if its form does not indicate time, then it is a noun. Among its properties is that judgment is upon it. And their saying 'min is a particle of jar' and 'daraba is a past tense verb' does not constitute a refutation against it, for it is a judgment upon the sound itself, i.e., the sound of 'min' and 'daraba', not upon their meaning. What is specific to the noun is this, i.e., judgment upon its meaning, for it is found in it and not found in others. But not absolutely; rather, if it is not conceived under the title of a nominal concept made as a mirror for observing it. And if its title is made a nominal concept, then judgment is upon it. So the meaning of 'min' is not independent, and it does not need this excuse—the one judging the nature in quantified expressions or their equivalent, but rather the one judging individuals. The first, i.e., judgment upon the sound itself, also occurs in meaningless words, like 'jasq' is meaningless.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the definition of the noun and its property. It begins by restating the condition: if a word's form does not indicate time, it is a noun. The key property mentioned is that judgment (hukm) applies to nouns. The author then addresses a potential objection: statements like 'min is a particle of jar' or 'daraba is a past tense verb' are judgments, but they appear to be about words that are particles and verbs, not nouns. The reply is that such judgments are on the sound or utterance itself (sawt), not on the meaning (ma'na). The property specific to nouns is judgment on meaning. The commentary further clarifies that for this property to hold, the noun must be considered under a nominal concept (mufhum ismi) as a mirror for its meaning; otherwise, if the concept itself is made the subject, judgment can still occur. The last point distinguishes between judging the nature (tabi'a) in quantified statements (like 'all men') and judging individuals; the noun's property relates to the latter. Finally, it notes that judgment on mere sound also applies to meaningless words (muhmalat) like 'jasq'. Throughout, the focus is on precision in logical analysis of language.

Key Terms
اسم
حكم
معنى
مهمل

Checkpoint

A) Judgment on the sound itself applies only to nouns.
B) Judgment on meaning is specific to nouns, while judgment on sound can apply to any word including meaningless ones.
C) Judgment on meaning applies to verbs and particles as well.
D) Judgment on sound is unique to meaningless words.

Key Takeaway
The noun's distinctive property is judgment upon its meaning, not upon its sound, and this is not contradicted by judgments that target the utterance itself.
